The Five Universals

Universal vs. Particular in External Existence
Author discusses the identity of universal and particular in external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a universal (kulli) and a particular (juz'i) in external existence, and explain why the universal is not identical to any particular individual.

Core Idea
The universal is a mental concept, not an external entity.

One of the foundational distinctions in Islamic logic is between the universal (kulli) and the particular (juz'i). A universal term, like 'human', can be predicated of many individuals, while a particular term, like 'Zayd', names a single individual. A common confusion is to think that the universal itself exists as an extra entity in the external world—but this passage clarifies that the universal is only a concept in the mind, not a concrete thing alongside individual particulars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being distinguished
Locate the terms 'kulli' (universal) and 'juz'i' (particular) in the passage. Note that the author is contrasting their existential status.
The terms are technical; 'kulli' refers to what can be predicated of many, 'juz'i' to what cannot.
2
Understand the core claim about external existence
The author asserts that the universal is not identical to any particular in the external world. For example, the universal 'human' is not the same as Zayd, even though Zayd is a human.
This refutes the misconception that the universal exists as a separate Platonic entity.
3
Apply the distinction to a concrete example
Consider the universal 'al-insan' (human) and the particular 'Zayd'. Zayd is a human, but the concept 'human' is not Zayd. The universal is a mental category that applies to Zayd and others.
This shows that universality is a property of concepts, not of external objects.
4
Check your understanding with a similar case
Take the universal 'al-hayawan' (animal). Is the animal-concept itself a particular animal? No—it is a universal that applies to many animals without being any one of them.
This reinforces that the universal's mode of existence is mental (dhihni), not external (khariji).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال زيد هو عين الحرم واع ترجيع ترجع المتوج اتحاده الا هو بالنسبة للتماسيح لا للمفهوم الي ان الرجل الشما ماع هو عين زيد في الخارج واللى كان في في الخارج اليف ونحال انه عينه الف فيه وانا جزيي وذاك كلي عن بعضهم مع
It is not said that Zayd is the same as the sanctuary? Rather he is Zayd in external existence, and what was in the external existence? and? that he is his same? so this is particular and that is universal from some of them with?
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Sullam addresses a logical distinction: the universal concept (kulli) is not identical to any particular individual (juz'i) in external existence. The example given appears to involve the name 'Zayd' and perhaps the term 'al-haram' (sanctuary). The author argues that although a universal term may be predicated of many individuals, the universal itself is not the same as any single individual in the outside world. This guards against the confusion that the universal has concrete existence like a particular. The passage seems to be part of a broader discussion on the five universals, specifically the relationship between genus or species and their instances.

Key Terms
كلي (kullī): Universal term predicated of many individuals; used in contrast to partial. [Note: No significant shift — stable across logical works.]
جزئي (juz'ī): Particular individual that cannot be predicated of many. [Note: No significant shift — stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between a universal and a particular in external existence?
A) The universal exists as a separate entity outside the mind.
B) The universal is identical to each particular it is predicated of.
C) The universal is a concept that is not identical to any particular external individual.
D) The particular is a part of the universal in external reality.

Key Takeaway
The universal is a mental concept that can be predicated of many particulars; it does not exist as an additional external entity alongside those particulars.

Dividing Words: Simple, Compound, Universal, Particular
Author divides words into simple/compound and universal/particular

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given Arabic word as simple or compound, and as universal or particular, using the criteria from this passage.

Core Idea
Words are either simple or compound; universals describe many, particulars describe one.

Every word we use in Islamic discourse — from Quranic terms to fiqh terminology — falls into basic logical categories. Before we can define anything, we need to know whether a word is simple (mufrad) or compound (murakkab), and whether its meaning is universal (kulli) or particular (juz'i). This lesson unpacks these foundational divisions as presented by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
The text says words are either compound (murakkab) or simple (mufrad). Note the criterion: a compound word has parts that each indicate a part of the meaning.
Example: 'Abdullah' is compound because 'Abd' and 'Allah' each contribute to the meaning.
2
Apply the compound criterion
Take a phrase like 'bismillah' and show how its parts (bi, ism, Allah) each indicate a part of the meaning. If you remove a part, the meaning changes.
3
Differentiate simple from compound
A simple word like 'asad' (lion) has parts (letters) that do not individually indicate meaning. The word is a single unit of meaning.
4
Understand the subtypes of simple words
The text says simple words are either universal (kulli) or particular (juz'i). Universal applies to many individuals (e.g., 'kitab' for any book); particular applies to a single individual (e.g., 'hadha al-kitab').
5
Test your classification
Classify these examples: 'Muhammad', 'rasul', 'dar al-harb', 'shajar' (tree). For each, decide mufrad/murakkab and kulli/juz'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ الأَلْفَاظِ) (٢٦) مُسْتَعْمَلُ الأَلْفَاظِ حَيْثُ يُوجَدُ : إِمَّا مُرَكَّبٌ، وَإِمَّا مُفْرَدُ (٢٧) فَأَوَّلٌ : مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزُءِ مَعْنَاهُ بِعَكْسِ مَا تَلَا (۲۸) وَهُوَ عَلَى قِسْمَيْنِ أَعْنِي : المُفْرَدًا - : كُلّي، اَوْ جُزْنِيُّ حَيْثُ وُجِدَا (۲۹) فَمُفْهِمُ اشْتِرَاكِ الكُلّيُّ» گـ: «أَسَدٍ»، وَعَكْسُهُ: «الجُزْئِيُّ» (٣٠) وَأَوَّلًا لِلذَّاتِ إِنْ فِيهَا انْدَرَج فَانْسِبْهُ، أَوْ لِعَارِضِ إِذَا خَرَجْ (۳۱) وَالكُلِّيَاتُ خَمْسَةٌ دُونَ انْتِقَاصُ : جِنْسٌ ، وَفَصْلٌ ، عَرَضُ، نَوْعٌ، وَخَاص (۳۲) وَأَوَّلُ ثَلَاثَةٌ بِلَا شَطَط جِنْسٌ قَرِيبٌ، أَوْ بَعِيدٌ، أَوْ وَسَط
Section on investigations of words: (26) Words used wherever found are either compound or simple. (27) The first (compound) is that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, unlike what follows (i.e., simple). (28) And it (simple) is of two types: I mean universal or particular wherever found. (29) What conveys commonality is universal like 'lion', and its opposite is particular. (30) And the first (universal) is either for the essence if it is included in it, so relate it, or for an accident if it goes out. (31) The universals are five without deficiency: genus, differentia, accident, species, and property. (32) And the first three (of genus), without deviation, are near genus, far genus, or intermediate.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage begins the section on words. Words are either compound (murakkab) or simple (mufrad). A compound word has parts that each indicate a part of the meaning (e.g., 'servant of Allah' has parts 'servant' and 'Allah'). A simple word does not. Simple words are either universal (kulli) applying to many like 'lion' or particular (juz'i) applying to one. The universal is either essential (dhati) if it is part of the essence of its instances, or accidental (aradi) if it is not. Then the author lists the five universals: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), accident (arad), species (naw'), and property (khas). Genus itself is divided into near, far, and intermediate.

Key Takeaway
The first step in logical analysis is to classify terms as simple or compound and as universal or particular.

The Five Universals
beginner
10 min
The Five Universals: Genus, Differentia, Species, Property, Accident
Author lists the five universals and subdivides genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five universals (jins, fasl, naw', khas, arad) and distinguish between near, far, and intermediate genus.

Core Idea
The five universals are the building blocks of all definitions in Islamic sciences.

After classifying words as universal or particular, the author presents the five types of universals: genus, differentia, species, property, and accident. These categories are essential for constructing precise definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm) in logic, theology, and law. This lesson unpacks each universal and explains how genus is further subdivided.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the list of five universals
The text explicitly states: 'The universals are five without deficiency: genus, differentia, accident, species, and property.' Commit this list to memory.
These are also called al-kulliyyat al-khams.
2
Understand genus (jins) and its subdivisions
Genus answers 'what is it?' and includes multiple species. The text divides genus into near (qarib), far (ba'id), and intermediate (wasat). For example, 'animal' is far genus for human, 'mammal' is intermediate, 'primate' is near.
Near genus shares the most commonality with the species; far genus is more abstract.
3
Identify differentia (fasl)
Differentia distinguishes a species from other species within the same genus. For humans, 'rational' is the differentia. It answers 'what kind?'
4
Define species (naw')
Species is the combination of genus and differentia, predicated of individuals. Example: 'human' = 'animal' + 'rational'. Species itself is a universal that applies to many individuals.
5
Distinguish property (khas) from accident (arad)
Property is a universal not part of the essence but unique to the species (e.g., 'laughing' for humans). Accident is also not essential but may be shared (e.g., 'walking'). Both are accidental universals (aradi).
The passage earlier says the universal is either essential (dhati) or accidental – property and accident fall under accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Five Universals
intermediate

Refining the Role of the Five Universals in Definitions
Anbabi refines the claim that the rules for conceptual knowledge are the five universals, clarifying their roles.

Learning Objective
At the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the five universals are directly used in forming definitions (hadd/rasm) and which are mentioned only by way of digression, and explain the reasoning behind this refinement.

Core Idea
Only three of the five universals—genus, differentia, and property—directly compose definitions.

In the previous lesson, you learned that the five universals are the foundation for conceptual definitions. However, as al-Anbabi points out, not all five play the same role. Some are essential for constructing definitions, while others are only tangentially relevant. This lesson will guide you through his critical refinement, so you can precisely understand the function of each universal in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed role of the five universals
The text states that the rules for conceptual knowledge are the rules of the five universals, because the means to the unknown (definition/description) is composed of them. Note the initial claim.
2
Locate the refinement: which two are excluded?
Al-Anbabi immediately adds that species (naw') and general accident ('arad 'amm) have no role in definitions; they are mentioned only by way of digression (istitrad). Underline this correction.
This is the key critical point.
3
Understand the reason for their exclusion
Only what is directly used to form the definition is relevant. Genus, differentia, and property are the actual building blocks. Species and general accident are not essential parts of a definition.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation of 'leading'
If 'leading' (isal) is interpreted broadly to include both the means and the terminus, then species becomes relevant because logic examines whether something is simple or composite. This shows the flexibility of the logical framework.
This is a secondary nuance, not the main point.
5
Note the role of the particular (juz'i)
The particular has no role in definitions (which are universal) but plays a role in syllogisms leading to assent (tasdiq), as the subject of a proposition. This connects to later topics in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنبابي
والقواعد الباحثةُ عن المعلومات التَّصوُّرِيَّة المتوقَّفُ عليها الموصل للمجهول التصوُّري هي القواعد المتعلقة بالكليات الخمس؛ التي هي: النوع، والعَرَضُ العام، والجنس، والفصل، والخاصة؛ وذلك لأنَّ الموصل الذي هو الحد أو الرسم متوقف على هذه الأمور؛ لأنه يتركب منها، لكن فيه أنَّ النوع لا دخل له في ذلك، وكذا العَرَض العام على ما اشتهر، فذكرهما إنما هو على سبيل الاستطراد.
نعم؛ مَن فسَّر الإيصال في عبارة من قال: من حيث الإيصال إلى مجهول بـ كونه موصلاً أو موصلاً إليه، حيث يبحث في المنطق عن كون البسيط لا يحد ولا يرسم، والمركب يحد ويرسم، جعلت مباحث النوع مقصودة بالذات داخلة في الإيصال لا فيما يتوقف عليه الإيصال، وذكر الجزئي على سبيل الاستطراد أيضاً؛ إذ لا دخل له في الحدود. نعم؛ له دخل في التوصيل إلى التصديق؛ إذ الجزئي يكون موضوعاً، وسيأتي أنَّ الموضوع معلومٌ تصوري يتوقف عليه القياس الموصل للتصديق بواسطة توقف القياس على جزئه، وهو القضية المركبة منه ومن المحمول.
Al-Anbabi: The rules that investigate conceptual knowledge which the means to the unknown conceptual depends on are the rules concerning the five universals, which are: species, general accident, genus, differentia, and property. This is because the means, which is the definition (hadd) or description (rasm), depends on these matters, since it is composed of them. But among them, species has no role in that, and likewise general accident according to what is well-known; so their mention is only by way of digression. Yes, whoever interpreted 'leading' (isal) in the phrase 'in terms of leading to the unknown' as being either a means or a terminus, such that in logic one investigates whether the simple is not defined or described, while the composite is defined and described – then the topics of species become intended per se, included in the leading (isal), not in what the leading depends on. And the mention of the particular is also by way of digression, since it has no role in definitions. Yes, it has a role in leading to assent (tasdiq), because the particular is the subject, and it will come that the subject is a conceptual known upon which the syllogism leading to assent depends, by virtue of the syllogism's dependence on its part, which is the proposition composed of it and the predicate.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by al-Anbabi (a hashiya commentator) regarding the five universals and their role in logic. He states that the rules for conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) are the rules of the five universals: species (naw'), general accident ('arad 'amm), genus (jins), differentia (fasl), and property (khassa). The reason given is that the 'means' (al-muwassil) to an unknown concept – i.e., definition (hadd) or description (rasm) – is composed of these universals. However, al-Anbabi immediately notes a critical point: species and general accident actually have no direct role in forming definitions; they are mentioned only by way of digression (istitrad). He then entertains an alternative interpretation: if one understands 'leading' (isal) broadly to include both the means and the terminus, then the investigation of species becomes directly relevant because logic examines whether something simple cannot be defined, while the composite can. In that case, species is part of the subject matter of logic. He also addresses the particular (juz'i): it has no role in definitions (which are universal), but it does play a role in syllogisms leading to assent, because it serves as the subject of a proposition. Thus, the passage refines the common claim that all five universals are equally foundational, emphasizing that only genus, differentia, and property are directly used in definitions, while species and general accident are included only incidentally. This is a typical hashiya-style correction that prevents students from misunderstanding the scope of the five universals.

Key Terms
الكليات الخمس
نوع
عرض عام
الموصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Anbabi, which two of the five universals are mentioned only by way of digression in the context of definitions?
A) Genus and differentia
B) Species and general accident
C) Property and differentia
D) Genus and species

Key Takeaway
Al-Anbabi's refinement shows that only genus, differentia, and property are directly operative in definitions; species and general accident are mentioned only incidentally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at the definition of 'salah' (prayer) as given by a faqih: 'Salah is a set of specific actions and sayings, beginning with takbir and ending with taslim, with intention.' Identify which of the five universals are used as genus and differentia, and which are not present.
?
In the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden,' the subject 'intoxicant' is a particular. Explain how the particular plays a role in the syllogism leading to assent (tasdiq), as mentioned by al-Anbabi.

The Five Universals
intermediate
12 min
The Five Universals: Essential vs. Digressive in Definition
Author lists the five universals and explains their necessity for definition (had/rasm).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the five universals are essential for definition (hadd/rasm) and which are digressive, and explain the role of species and common accident in the broader logical framework.

Core Idea
Not all five universals construct definitions — species and common accident are digressive.

The five universals are foundational to conceptual knowledge in logic. However, not all of them are directly used in crafting definitions (hadd) or descriptions (rasm). Our passage clarifies that species (naw') and common accident ('arad 'amm) are mentioned only by way of digression (istitrad), while genus, differentia, and property are the primary building blocks. This lesson unpacks this distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five universals
Find the list in the Arabic passage: النوع، العرض العام، الجنس، الفصل، الخاصة. Translate each: species, common accident, genus, differentia, property.
These are the kulliyat khams that form the basis for definition and description.
2
Distinguish essential from digressive
The text says the conduit (definition/description) is composed from these universals, but then notes an objection: species and common accident have no real role in definition. Their mention is digressive (istitrad). Understand that genus and differentia are essential for hadd (complete definition), while property and common accident are used for rasm (description).
Digression does not mean useless; it means they are not directly part of the composition of a definition.
3
Understand the resolution
The author offers a reinterpretation: if 'conduction' (īṣāl) is understood broadly to include both what conducts and what is conducted to, then species becomes relevant because logic studies which things are simple (cannot be defined) and which are composite (can be defined). Thus species, as a composite, enters the discussion not as a component of definition but as the subject of definition.
This shows how the scope of logic encompasses both definition and the things being defined.
4
Recognize the role of the particular (juz'i)
The particular is also digressive in definition, but it plays a crucial role in tasdiq (assenting). In a syllogism, the subject is a particular concept, and the syllogism depends on propositions composed of subject and predicate. So the particular is essential for the conduit to assent.
This ties conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) to assenting knowledge (tasdiq) through the syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقواعد الباحثة عن المعلومات التصوريَّة المتوقف عليها الموصل للمجهول التصوري هي القواعد المتعلقة بالكليات الخمس؛ التي هي : النَّوع ، والعَرَضُ العام، والجنس، والفصل، والخاصة؛ وذلك لأنَّ الموصل الذي هو الحد أو الرَّسم متوقف على هذه الأمور؛ لأنه يتركب منها، لكن فيه أنَّ النوع لا دخل له في ذلك، وكذا العَرَض العام على ما اشتهر، فذكرهما إنّما هو على سبيل الاستطراد. نعم؛ مَن فسَّر الإيصال في عبارة من قال: من حيث الإيصال إلى مجهول بـ كونه موصلاً أو موصلاً إليه، حيث يبحث في المنطق عن كون البسيط لا يحد ولا يرسم، والمركب يحد ويرسم، جعلت مباحث النَّوع مقصودة بالذات داخلة في الإيصال لا فيما يتوقف عليه الإيصال، وذكر الجزئي على سبيل الاستطراد أيضاً؛ إذ لا دخل له في الحدود. نعم؛ له دخل في التوصيل إلى التصديق؛ إذ الجزئي يكون موضوعاً، وسيأتي أنَّ الموضوع معلومٌ تصوري يتوقف عليه القياس الموصل للتصديق بواسطة توقف القياس على جزئه، وهو القضيَّة المركّبة منه ومِنَ المحمول.
And the rules investigating the conceptual information (tasawwuriyyāt) on which the conduit to the unknown conceptual depends are the rules related to the five universals, which are: species (al-naw'), common accident (al-'arad al-'amm), genus (al-jins), differentia (al-fasl), and property (al-khassa). This is because the conduit, which is either definition (hadd) or description (rasm), depends on these matters since it is composed from them. However, there is [the objection] that species has no role in that, and likewise common accident, as is well-known, so their mention is only by way of digression. Yes: whoever explains 'conduction' in the statement 'from the perspective of conduction to the unknown' as meaning either what conducts or that to which conduction is made—where logic investigates that the simple is not defined nor described, while the composite is defined and described—makes the investigations of species essentially intended, included in the conduction, not in what depends on the conduction. And the mention of the particular (al-juz'i) is also by way of digression, since it has no role in definitions. Yes, it has a role in conduction to assenting (tasdiq); for the particular is a subject, and it will come that the subject is known conceptual information on which the syllogism leading to assenting depends, due to the dependence of the syllogism on its part, which is the proposition composed of the subject and the predicate.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines and justifies the inclusion of the five universals in logic. The author states that the rules for conceptual (tasawwuriyyāt) information—which are necessary for defining or describing an unknown concept—are the rules of the five universals: species, common accident, genus, differentia, and property. The reason is that a definition (hadd) or description (rasm) is composed from these universals. However, a common objection is noted: species and common accident have no real role in definition, so their inclusion is digressive (istitrad). The author then offers a solution: if 'conduction' is interpreted broadly to include both the thing that conducts and the thing to which one is conducted, then species becomes directly relevant because logic studies which things are simple (unable to be defined) and which are composite (capable of definition). Thus, species (as a composite) enters the discussion directly. Similarly, the particular (juz'i) is only digressive in definitions, but it plays a crucial role in assenting (tasdiq) because it serves as the subject of propositions in syllogisms. This shows the interplay between conceptual and assertoric inquiry, as the subject is a known concept that supports the syllogism yielding assent.

الحد
الرسم
الاستطراد

Key Takeaway
Only genus, differentia, property, and common accident are directly used in definitions and descriptions; species and common accident are digressive but become relevant when considering what can be defined.

The Five Universals

Defining Universal and Particular Terms
Author defines universal as signifying participation and particular as its opposite, then defends the definition against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish universal from particular terms using the logicians' criterion of predication, and defend this definition against the objection of shared proper names.

Core Idea
A universal is not just anything shared; it must be truly predicable of many.

In logic, knowing whether a term is universal or particular is foundational for constructing valid definitions and arguments. The author defines the universal as 'the signifier of participation' and the particular as its opposite. But an objector raises a problem: a proper name like 'Zaid' is shared among his children, yet it is a particular. The author resolves this by clarifying that 'shared' in the logicians' sense means 'predicable of many', not merely related. Let's walk through this analysis step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and example
The author states that the universal is 'the signifier of participation' (mufhim al-ishtirak) and gives 'lion' as an example. Notice that 'lion' can be truthfully said of many individual lions.
2
Understand the objection
The objector argues: if 'signifier of participation' means 'shared', then the proper name 'Zaid' is shared among his children (since he is their father), yet Zaid is a particular. So the definition would wrongly include some particulars.
The objector's point is that the definition is not restrictive (mani').
3
Recognize the key distinction
The author responds that 'shared' in logic specifically means 'true of many in the sense of being predicated' (yasduqu 'ala kathirin bi ma'na yasihhu hamlihi 'alayha). Zaid is shared in terms of paternity (relation), but he cannot be predicated of his children (you don't say 'Zaid is his son'). So the definition stands.
The key is the type of sharing: predication (haml) vs. relation (nisba).
4
Apply the criterion to new examples
Test terms: 'Muhammad' as a proper name is not predicable of many in a universal sense (though many are named Muhammad, the term is not predicated equally because each 'Muhammad' is a different individual). 'Human' is predicable of all humans, so it is a universal.
This clarifies the logical concept of universality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ الْأَلْفَاظِ. وَهُوَ عَلَى قِسْمَيْنِ: أَعْنِي الْمُفْرَدَ: كُلِّيًّا أَوْ جُزْئِيًّا حَيْثُ وُجِدَا. فَمُفْهِمُ اشْتِرَاكِ الْكُلِّيُّ كَـ: «أَسَدٍ»، وَعَكْسُهُ: الْجُزْئِيُّ. لَا يُقَالُ: «مُفْهِمُ الْاِشْتِرَاكِ» عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ، فَكَأَنَّهُ قَالَ: فَالْكُلِّيُّ هُوَ الْمُشْتَرَكُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَصْدُقُ بِـ زَيْدٍ الَّذِي اشْتَرَكَ فِيهِ بَنُوهُ مَثَلًا، لِأَنَّهُ مُشْتَرَكٌ بَيْنَهُمْ مِنْ حَيْثُ أُبُوَّتِهِ لَهُمْ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ جُزْئِيٌّ، فَيَكُونُ التَّعْرِيفُ غَيْرَ مَانِعٍ. لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ: الْمُرَادُ بِالْمُشْتَرَكِ مَا جَرَى عَلَيْهِ اصْطِلَاحُ الْمَنَاطِقَةِ، وَهُوَ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ، بِمَعْنَى أَنَّهُ يَصِحُّ حَمْلُهُ عَلَيْهَا، وَمَا ذُكِرَ لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ; لِأَنَّهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَ بَنِيهِ بِاعْتِبَارِ أُبُوَّتِهِ لَهُمْ، لَكِنْ لَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِمْ بِالْمَعْنَى الْمَذْكُورِ.
A section on discussions of words. It (the single term) is of two divisions: the single term: universal or particular, wherever they are found. So the signifier of participation is the universal, like 'lion', and its opposite is the particular. It should not be said: 'Signifier of participation' is an expression for the shared (term), so it is as if he said: the universal is the shared (term). Then it would apply to Zaid, in whom his sons share, for example, because he is shared among them in terms of his paternity to them, despite him being particular. So the definition would not be restrictive. Because we say: What is meant by 'shared' is that which the logicians' convention applies to, and it is that which is true of many, meaning that it is validly predicated of them. What was mentioned is not like that; because although he is shared among his sons in consideration of his paternity to them, he is not true of them in the mentioned sense.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage defines the universal and particular terms and defends the definition against a potential objection. It begins by stating that the single term (mufrad) is divided into universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i). The universal is defined as 'what signifies participation' (mufhim ishtirak), exemplified by 'lion' which applies to many individual lions. The particular is simply its opposite. The objector then raises a problem: if 'signifier of participation' is just another phrase for 'shared' (mushtarak), then the definition would incorrectly include proper names like 'Zaid'. Zaid is shared among his children as their father, yet Zaid is a particular individual, not a universal — so the definition would not be restrictive (mani'). The response clarifies that the logicians' term 'shared' specifically means 'true of many' (yadiqu 'ala kathirin) in the sense of being predicated (haml) of them, not merely being related to them in some way. Zaid, while shared in terms of paternity, cannot be predicated of his children (one does not say 'Zaid is his son' in a predicative sense), so the definition stands. A key subtlety is that the universal must be predicable equally (haml muwata'a), not just related via derivation (haml ishtiqaq). Thus, 'lion' is predicated of each lion; 'Zaid' is not predicated of his sons.

Key Terms
مُفْهِمُ اشْتِرَاكِ (mufhim al-ishtirak): The signifier of participation; the author defines the universal as that which signifies commonality among multiple individuals. [Note: This phrasing is specific to this commentary; other texts define kulli simply as 'ma yasduqu 'ala kathirin'.]
كُلِّي (kulli): Universal: a term that can be truly predicated of many individuals, like 'lion' applied to many lions. [Note: Stable across the tradition, though later logicians distinguish five types (jins, naw', etc.).]
جُزْئِي (juz'i): Particular: a term that applies to only one individual, like a proper name. [Note: Stable.]
مُشْتَرَك (mushtarak): Shared; here it means a term that is true of many in the logical sense of predicability, not just related. [Note: In Arabic grammar, mushtarak often refers to homonyms; here it is used in the logical context of predication.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The universal is defined by predication, not mere sharing; proper names are not universals even if shared among people.

The Five Universals

The Sixfold Division of Universals by Later Logicians
Commentator presents the sixfold division of universals by later logicians, with examples and refinements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal into one of the six types based on the criteria of existence and finitude of individuals.

Core Idea
Later logicians refined the threefold division into six types by distinguishing impossibility, uniqueness, and infinity of instances.

The earlier classification of universals divided them into those with no instances, one instance, and multiple instances. Later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) expanded this into six types by further subdividing each category. This lesson presents that sixfold division with examples from Islamic theology and cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three primary categories from the ancients
Before the later scholars, universals were divided into three: those with no existing individuals, those with one existing individual, and those with multiple existing individuals. The later scholars built on this.
This threefold division is not explicitly in the passage but is background.
2
Examine the subdivision of the first category
The first category (no individuals) is split into (1) that whose existence is impossible, like 'combination of opposites', and (2) that which is possible but does not happen to exist, like 'a sea of mercury'.
3
Examine the subdivision of the second category
The second category (one individual) is split into (3) that which cannot have another individual besides the one that exists, like 'the divine' (Allah), and (4) that which could have another but does not, like 'the sun' in the Ptolemaic system.
4
Examine the subdivision of the third category
The third category (multiple individuals) is split into (5) those with infinitely many individuals, like 'divine attributes' (knowledge, power), and (6) those with finitely many individuals, like 'planets'.
5
Note the three subcategories under finite individuals
Under type (6) there are three: (a) only those finite individuals exist (e.g., known planets), (b) additional individuals exist that are infinite (e.g., blessings of Allah, but commentator rejects), (c) additional individuals exist that are finite (e.g., 'lion').
The commentator notes that the detailed classification yields eight types total.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَجَعَلَهَا المُتَأَخِّرُونَ سِتَةٌ ; حَيْثُ قَسَمُوا الأَوَّلَ إِلَى :
(۱) - مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ وُجُودُ شَيْءٍ مِنْهُ ; كَـ : «الجَمْعِ بَيْنَ الضَّدَّيْنِ »
(۲) - وَمَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ ; كَـ : بَحْرٍ مِنْ زِثْبَقٍ ) .
وَقَسَمُوا الثَّانِي إِلَى : 
(۳) ـ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ وُجُودُ غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ الفَرْدِ الَّذِي وُجِدَ مِنْهُ ; كَـ : «الإِلَهِ» .
(٤) ـ وَمَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ ; كَـ: «الشَّمْسِ».
وَقَسَّمُوا الثَّالِثَ إِلَى : 
(٥) - ما وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادُ غَيْرُ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ ; كَـ : «الصَّفَةِ »، فَإِنَّ أَفْرَادَهَا الَّتِي وُجِدَتْ لَا تَتَنَاهَى ; لأَنَّ مِنْهَا الصِّفَاتِ الوُجُودِيَّةَ القَائِمَةَ بِذَاتِهِ تَعَالَى، وَقَدْ دَلَّ الدَّلِيلُ عَلَى أَنَّهَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا ، وَاسْتِحَالَةُ وُجُودِ مَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهُ إِنَّمَا ثَبَتَتْ فِي حَقٌّ الحَوَادِثِ، وَلَا يَصِحُ التَّمْثِيلُ لِذَلِكَ بِ: نِعْمَةِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى كَمَا صَنَعَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ ; لأَنَّ الكَلَامَ فِيمَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادُ لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا ، وَنِعْمَةُ اللهِ تَعَالَى لَيْسَتْ كَذَلِكَ.
نَعَمْ؛ هِيَ لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا ; بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ مَا مِنْ نِعْمَةٍ إِلَّا وَبَعْدَهَا نِعْمَةٌ ، .. وَهَكَذَا، وَلَيْسَ ذَلِكَ مُرَاداً هُنَا، وَلَا يَصِحُ أَيْضاً التَّمْثِيلُ لِذَلِكَ بِحَرَكَةِ الفَلَكِ; لأَنَّهُ لَا يَتَمَشَّى إِلَّا عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الفَلَاسِفَةُ مِنْ أَنَّهُ مَا مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ إِلَّا وَقَبْلَهَا حَرَكَةٌ .. وَهَكَذَا إِلَى مَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهُ فِي جَانِبِ المَاضِي، وَيَبْنُونَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ أَنَّهَا قَدِيمَةٌ بِالنَّوْعِ حَادِثَةٌ بِالشَّخْصِ ، وَهُوَ مَذْهَبٌ بَاطِلٌ، وَمُعْتَقِدُهُ كَافِرٌ.
(٦) - وَمَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادٌ مُتَنَاهِيَةٌ، وَتَحْتَ هَذَا القِسْمِ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْسَام : 
- مَا لَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادُ سِوَى تِلْكَ الأَفْرَادِ المُتَنَاهِيَةِ ; كَـ : «الكَوَاكِبِ »
- وَمَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادٌ سِوَاهَا ، وَهِيَ غَيْرُ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ ; كَ: نِعْمَةِ اللهِ تَعَالَى».
- وَمَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادٌ سِوَاهَا وَهِيَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٌ، وَهُوَ مَا مَثَّلَ لَهُ المُصَنِّفُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «كَ : أَسَدٍ»، فَفِي الحَقِيقَةِ تَؤولُ الأَقْسَامُ إِلَى ثَمَانِيَةٍ تَفْصِيلاً، وَبِهَذَا سَقَط مَا لِبَعْضِهِمْ هُنَا، فَاحْفَظْ ذَلِكَ. (وعلى هذا النحو يُفهم أن التقسيم التفصيلي ثمانية أقسام)
And the later scholars made them six, by dividing the first (category) into: (1) that whose existence of any individual is impossible, like 'the combination of opposites'; (2) and that which is not impossible, like 'a sea of mercury'. And they divided the second into: (3) that whose existence of any other individual besides the one that exists is impossible, like 'the divine'; (4) and that which is not impossible, like 'the sun'. And they divided the third into: (5) that from which individuals exist that are infinite, like 'attribute' – for its individuals that exist are without end, because among them are the existential attributes subsisting in His essence (Exalted is He), and the proof has indicated that they have no end, and the impossibility of the existence of what has no end is only established with respect to occurrences (hawadith), and it is not correct to illustrate that with 'the blessing of Allah the Exalted' as some have done, because the discussion is about what has existing individuals with no end, and the blessing of Allah the Exalted is not like that. Yes, they are without end in the sense that there is no blessing except that after it there is a blessing, and so on, but that is not intended here. Nor is it correct to illustrate that with the motion of the celestial sphere, because it only applies according to what the philosophers hold – that there is no motion except that before it there is a motion, and so on to infinity in the past, and they build on that that it is eternal in type and temporally originated in individual, and that is a false doctrine, and its believer is a disbeliever. (6) And that from which finite individuals exist, and under this division are three divisions: - that which has no individuals other than those finite ones, like 'the planets'; - and that which has individuals other than them, and they are infinite, like 'the blessing of Allah the Exalted'; - and that which has individuals other than them and they are finite, and that is what the author exemplified with saying 'like lion'. So in reality the divisions amount to eight in detail, and by this what some have said here falls, so memorize that.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage expands the threefold division of the universal into six by the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun). They took the first category (no existing individuals) and split it into two: (1) what is impossible to exist, like the combination of opposites (a logical impossibility), and (2) what is possible but does not happen to exist, like a sea of mercury. The second category (only one existing individual) is divided into (3) what cannot have another individual besides the one that exists, like Allah (since there cannot be another god), and (4) what could have another but as a matter of fact has only one, like the sun in the Ptolemaic system. The third category (multiple individuals) is divided into (5) those with infinitely many individuals, like the divine attributes (e.g., knowledge, power) which are known to be infinite in number, and (6) those with finitely many individuals, like planets. Under (6), there are three subcategories: (a) only those finite individuals exist, like the known planets; (b) additional individuals exist that are infinite, like blessings of Allah (but the commentator argues this is not apt because blessings are finite in actual existence? Actually, the commentator says blessings are infinite in the sense of sequential but not in simultaneous existence); and (c) additional individuals exist that are finite, like the universal 'lion' which has a finite number of individual lions. The commentator notes that the detailed classification actually yields eight types, correcting a misconception of some scholars.

Key Terms
المتأخرون
الصفة
يستحيل

Key Takeaway
The sixfold division of universals is based on the impossibility, finitude, and number of existing individuals.

The Five Universals
intermediate
8 min
The Commentator's Correction of Examples for Infinite Universals
Commentator refines the example for infinite universals, rejecting blessings and motion as invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which examples are valid for an infinite universal and explain the commentator's reasoning.

Core Idea
The commentator rejects 'blessings of Allah' and 'celestial motion' as examples of infinite universals because they involve sequential infinity, not actual simultaneous existence.

In the sixfold division, type (5) covers universals with infinitely many existing individuals. The commentator addresses two proposed examples from others—'blessings of Allah' and 'motion of the sphere'—and explains why they are incorrect. This lesson unpacks his critique.

Guided Path
1
Recall type (5): infinite existing individuals
Type (5) is a universal from which infinitely many individuals exist simultaneously, like the divine attributes.
2
Examine the first rejected example: blessings of Allah
Some scholars proposed 'blessings of Allah' as an example. The commentator explains that blessings are infinite only in a sequential sense (one after another), not as simultaneous existents. Therefore, they do not fit type (5).
The commentator says 'the discussion is about what has existing individuals with no end' meaning actual existents.
3
Examine the second rejected example: motion of the celestial sphere
This example relies on the philosophical claim that motion is infinite in the past. The commentator rejects this as a false doctrine (madhhab batil) and states that its believer is a disbeliever (kafir). Hence it cannot be a valid example.
4
Identify the correct example: divine attributes
The commentator affirms that divine attributes (like knowledge, power) are infinite in number and subsist in Allah's essence. This is established by proof and fits the category perfectly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَجَعَلَهَا المُتَأَخِّرُونَ سِتَةٌ ; حَيْثُ قَسَمُوا الأَوَّلَ إِلَى :
(۱) - مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ وُجُودُ شَيْءٍ مِنْهُ ; كَـ : «الجَمْعِ بَيْنَ الضَّدَّيْنِ »
(۲) - وَمَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ ; كَـ : بَحْرٍ مِنْ زِثْبَقٍ ) .
وَقَسَمُوا الثَّانِي إِلَى : 
(۳) ـ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ وُجُودُ غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ الفَرْدِ الَّذِي وُجِدَ مِنْهُ ; كَـ : «الإِلَهِ» .
(٤) ـ وَمَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ ; كَـ: «الشَّمْسِ».
وَقَسَّمُوا الثَّالِثَ إِلَى : 
(٥) - ما وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادُ غَيْرُ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ ; كَـ : «الصَّفَةِ »، فَإِنَّ أَفْرَادَهَا الَّتِي وُجِدَتْ لَا تَتَنَاهَى ; لأَنَّ مِنْهَا الصِّفَاتِ الوُجُودِيَّةَ القَائِمَةَ بِذَاتِهِ تَعَالَى، وَقَدْ دَلَّ الدَّلِيلُ عَلَى أَنَّهَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا ، وَاسْتِحَالَةُ وُجُودِ مَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهُ إِنَّمَا ثَبَتَتْ فِي حَقٌّ الحَوَادِثِ، وَلَا يَصِحُ التَّمْثِيلُ لِذَلِكَ بِ: نِعْمَةِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى كَمَا صَنَعَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ ; لأَنَّ الكَلَامَ فِيمَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادُ لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا ، وَنِعْمَةُ اللهِ تَعَالَى لَيْسَتْ كَذَلِكَ.
نَعَمْ؛ هِيَ لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا ; بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ مَا مِنْ نِعْمَةٍ إِلَّا وَبَعْدَهَا نِعْمَةٌ ، .. وَهَكَذَا، وَلَيْسَ ذَلِكَ مُرَاداً هُنَا، وَلَا يَصِحُ أَيْضاً التَّمْثِيلُ لِذَلِكَ بِحَرَكَةِ الفَلَكِ; لأَنَّهُ لَا يَتَمَشَّى إِلَّا عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الفَلَاسِفَةُ مِنْ أَنَّهُ مَا مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ إِلَّا وَقَبْلَهَا حَرَكَةٌ .. وَهَكَذَا إِلَى مَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهُ فِي جَانِبِ المَاضِي، وَيَبْنُونَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ أَنَّهَا قَدِيمَةٌ بِالنَّوْعِ حَادِثَةٌ بِالشَّخْصِ ، وَهُوَ مَذْهَبٌ بَاطِلٌ، وَمُعْتَقِدُهُ كَافِرٌ.
(٦) - وَمَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادٌ مُتَنَاهِيَةٌ، وَتَحْتَ هَذَا القِسْمِ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْسَام : 
- مَا لَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادُ سِوَى تِلْكَ الأَفْرَادِ المُتَنَاهِيَةِ ; كَـ : «الكَوَاكِبِ »
- وَمَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادٌ سِوَاهَا ، وَهِيَ غَيْرُ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ ; كَ: نِعْمَةِ اللهِ تَعَالَى».
- وَمَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادٌ سِوَاهَا وَهِيَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٌ، وَهُوَ مَا مَثَّلَ لَهُ المُصَنِّفُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «كَ : أَسَدٍ»، فَفِي الحَقِيقَةِ تَؤولُ الأَقْسَامُ إِلَى ثَمَانِيَةٍ تَفْصِيلاً، وَبِهَذَا سَقَط مَا لِبَعْضِهِمْ هُنَا، فَاحْفَظْ ذَلِكَ. (وعلى هذا النحو يُفهم أن التقسيم التفصيلي ثمانية أقسام)
And the later scholars made them six, by dividing the first (category) into: (1) that whose existence of any individual is impossible, like 'the combination of opposites'; (2) and that which is not impossible, like 'a sea of mercury'. And they divided the second into: (3) that whose existence of any other individual besides the one that exists is impossible, like 'the divine'; (4) and that which is not impossible, like 'the sun'. And they divided the third into: (5) that from which individuals exist that are infinite, like 'attribute' – for its individuals that exist are without end, because among them are the existential attributes subsisting in His essence (Exalted is He), and the proof has indicated that they have no end, and the impossibility of the existence of what has no end is only established with respect to occurrences (hawadith), and it is not correct to illustrate that with 'the blessing of Allah the Exalted' as some have done, because the discussion is about what has existing individuals with no end, and the blessing of Allah the Exalted is not like that. Yes, they are without end in the sense that there is no blessing except that after it there is a blessing, and so on, but that is not intended here. Nor is it correct to illustrate that with the motion of the celestial sphere, because it only applies according to what the philosophers hold – that there is no motion except that before it there is a motion, and so on to infinity in the past, and they build on that that it is eternal in type and temporally originated in individual, and that is a false doctrine, and its believer is a disbeliever. (6) And that from which finite individuals exist, and under this division are three divisions: - that which has no individuals other than those finite ones, like 'the planets'; - and that which has individuals other than them, and they are infinite, like 'the blessing of Allah the Exalted'; - and that which has individuals other than them and they are finite, and that is what the author exemplified with saying 'like lion'. So in reality the divisions amount to eight in detail, and by this what some have said here falls, so memorize that.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage expands the threefold division of the universal into six by the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun). They took the first category (no existing individuals) and split it into two: (1) what is impossible to exist, like the combination of opposites (a logical impossibility), and (2) what is possible but does not happen to exist, like a sea of mercury. The second category (only one existing individual) is divided into (3) what cannot have another individual besides the one that exists, like Allah (since there cannot be another god), and (4) what could have another but as a matter of fact has only one, like the sun in the Ptolemaic system. The third category (multiple individuals) is divided into (5) those with infinitely many individuals, like the divine attributes (e.g., knowledge, power) which are known to be infinite in number, and (6) those with finitely many individuals, like planets. Under (6), there are three subcategories: (a) only those finite individuals exist, like the known planets; (b) additional individuals exist that are infinite, like blessings of Allah (but the commentator argues this is not apt because blessings are finite in actual existence? Actually, the commentator says blessings are infinite in the sense of sequential but not in simultaneous existence); and (c) additional individuals exist that are finite, like the universal 'lion' which has a finite number of individual lions. The commentator notes that the detailed classification actually yields eight types, correcting a misconception of some scholars.

الصفة
يستحيل
المتأخرون

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the 'motion of the celestial sphere' not a valid example of an infinite universal?
A) It has only finite individuals.
B) It is based on a false philosophical doctrine.
C) It is not a universal.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
For a universal to have infinite individuals, those individuals must exist simultaneously in actuality, not merely in sequence or in a false cosmological theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example of a universal that has infinitely many simultaneous existing individuals from Islamic theology, explaining why it fits type (5) and not the rejected examples.
?
Why did the commentator reject 'blessings of Allah' as an example? Distinguish between sequential and simultaneous infinity.

The Five Universals
beginner
5 min
The Universal and Particular: Core Definitions
Defines universal and particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify terms as universal or particular based on the criterion of conceptual sharing (al-ishtirak al-ma'nawi).

Core Idea
Universal conveys conceptual sharing; particular does not.

In Islamic logic, every term is either universal or particular. This distinction is foundational for definitions and arguments. The passage gives the standard definitions and clarifies that only conceptual sharing counts, not verbal homonymy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of universal
The universal is defined as 'what conveys sharing' (mufhim ishtirak). This means its meaning applies to multiple individuals, like 'lion' applying to many lions. Note that sharing must be conceptual (ma'nawi), not verbal (lafzi).
Verbal sharing would be like 'ayn meaning both eye and spring; that is not intended.
2
Understand the definition of particular
The particular is the opposite: it does not convey sharing. Its meaning applies to only one individual, like 'Zaid' referring to a specific person. Accidental verbal sharing (e.g., two persons named Zaid) is disregarded because only conceptual sharing matters.
3
Apply the definitions to examples
Test a term: Does its very concept (mafhūm) apply to more than one individual? If yes, it is universal; if no, it is particular. For instance, 'human' is universal because the concept applies to many; 'this pen' is particular.
The passage uses 'lion' as a universal example and 'Zaid' as a particular example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَمُفْهِمُ اشْتِرَاكِ «الكُلّيُّ» كَ: «أَسَدٍ»، وَعَكْسُهُ: «الجُزْئِيُّ». نَعَمْ؛ هِيَ لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا (١) ; بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ مَا مِنْ نِعْمَةٍ إِلَّا وَبَعْدَهَا نِعْمَةٌ ، .. وَهَكَذَا، وَلَيْسَ ذَلِكَ مُرَاداً هُنَا، وَلَا يَصِحُ أَيْضاً التَّمْثِيلُ لِذَلِكَ بِحَرَكَةِ الفَلَكِ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا يَتَمَشَّى إِلَّا عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الفَلَاسِفَةُ مِنْ أَنَّهُ (٢) مَا مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ إِلَّا وَقَبْلَهَا حَرَكَةٌ .. وَهَكَذَا إِلَى مَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهُ فِي جَانِبِ المَاضِي (٣) , وَيَبْنُونَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ أَنَّهَا قَدِيمَةٌ بِالنَّوْعِ حَادِثَةٌ بِالشَّخْصِ , وَهُوَ مَذْهَبٌ بَاطِلٌ، وَمُعْتَقِدُهُ كَافِرٌ. (٦) - وَمَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادٌ مُتَنَاهِيَةٌ، وَتَحْتَ هَذَا القِسْمِ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْسَام : - مَا لَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادُ سِوَى تِلْكَ الأَفْرَادِ المُتَنَاهِيَةِ ; كَـ : «الكَوَاكِبِ (٤) - - وَمَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادٌ سِوَاهَا , وَهِيَ غَيْرُ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ ; كَ: نِعْمَةِ اللهِ تَعَالَى». - وَمَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادٌ سِوَاهَا وَهِيَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٌ، وَهُوَ مَا مَثَّلَ لَهُ المُصَنِّفُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «كَ : أَسَدٍ», فَفِي الحَقِيقَةِ تَؤولُ الأَقْسَامُ إِلَى ثَمَانِيَةٍ تَفْصِيلاً، وَبِهَذَا سَقَط مَا لِبَعْضِهِمْ هُنَا، فَاحْفَظْ ذَلِكَ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَعَكْسُهُ : «الجُزْئِيُّ» فَهُوَ مَا لَا يُفْهِمُ الاشْتِرَاكَ كَـ: «زَيْدِ», فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُفْهِمُ الاشْتِرَاكَ. وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِمَا يَعْرِضُ لَهُ مِنَ الاشْتِرَاكِ اللَّفْظِيّ; لِمَا تَقَدَّمَ مِنْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ هُنَا : الاشْتِرَاكُ المَعْنَوِيُّ. وَإِنَّمَا قَدَّمَ المُصَنِّفُ تَعْرِيفَ الكُلِّيِّ عَلَى تَعْرِيفِ الجُزْئِيِّ : ١ - اهْتِمَاماً بِهِ، لِكَوْنِهِ مَادَّةَ الحُدُودِ دَائِماً , وَالبَرَاهِينِ وَالمَطَالِبِ غَالِباً (٥). الأنبابي قوله : (مَادَّةَ الحُدُودِ دَائِماً , وَالبَرَاهِينِ أراد بـ «الحدود» : مطلق التعاريف، وبـ «البراهين»: مطلق الأقيسة، ففي كلامه تغليب [ب/ ٢٤] . أو المراد : الحدود الحقيقيَّة والبراهين الحقيقية، فيكون تخصيصهما بالذكر; لأشرفيتهما . وقوله : (وَالمَطَالِبِ ) هي النتائج ; لأنَّها تطلب بالدليل . اهـ (صبَّان (٦). (1) الشرشيمي : قوله : (نَعَمْ ; هِي ... إلخ) استدراك , ونعمة الله تعالى ليست كذلك، فإنَّه يُوهِم أنَّها متناهية حتَّى الأفراد غير الموجودة . (۲) في بعض النسخ الخطية: «بأنه» بدلاً من «من أنه» . (3) الشرشيمي : قوله : ( فِي جَانِبِ المَاضِي أي : وما من حركة إلَّا وبعدها حركة في جانب المستقبل . (٤) في المطبوع: «الكوكب بدلاً من «الكواكب». (0) الشرشيمي : قوله : ( مَادَّةَ الحُدُودِ) أي : لأنَّها لا تكون إلَّا كلِّيَّةٌ ; سواء كانت بالذاتيَّات أو بالعَرَضيَّات. وقوله : (غالباً) أي : لأنَّه قد يدخل الجزئي في البراهين والمطالب التي هي النتائج ; كأن قال : «زَيْدٌ مُتَكَلِّمْ، وَكُلُّ مُتَكَلِّمِ إِنْسَانٌ» فينتج : «زَيْدٌ إِنْسَانٌ» . (٦) انظر : حاشية الصبان على الشرح الصغير للملوي (ص: ٢٥٥). وَلِأَنَّهُ قَدْ عَرَّفَ الكُلِّيَّ بِالإِيجَابِ، وَالجُزْئِيَّ بِالسَّلْبِ، وَالإِيجَابُ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ السَّلْبِ، وَأَيْضاً : سَلْبُ الشَّيْءِ لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ إِلَّا بَعْدَ تَعَقُلِ وُجُودِهِ. وَبِالوَجْهِ الأَوَّلِ يُوَجَّهُ تَقْدِيمُ غَيْرِ المُصَنِّفِ لِذَلِكَ , لَا بِالوَجْهِ الثَّانِي ; لِأَنَّ غَيْرَ الْمُصَنِّفِ إِنَّمَا عَرَّفَ الكُلِّيَّ بِالسَّلْبِ حَيْثُ قَالَ: مَا لَا يَمْنَعُ نَفْسُ تَصَوُّرِهِ مِنْ وُقُوعِ الشَّرِكَةِ فِيهِ (۱) وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ كَلَامَ المُصَنِّفِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الجُزْنِيِّ الحَقِيقِيِّ , وَأَمَّا الجُزْنِيُّ الإِضَافِيُّ فَهُوَ : «مَا انْدَرَجَ تَحْتَ مَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ مِنْهُ , وَبَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الحَقِيقِيّ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ، فَيَجْتَمِعَانِ فِي «زَيْدِ» مَثَلاً، وَيَنْفَرِدُ الإِضَافِيُّ فِي نَحْوِ الإِنْسَانِ».
The universal is what conveys sharing, such as 'lion'; and its opposite is the particular. Yes, they have no limit; meaning: there is no blessing but after it is a blessing, and so on, and that is not intended here. Nor is it correct to exemplify that by the movement of the celestial sphere, because it only applies according to what the philosophers hold: that there is no movement but before it a movement, and so on to infinity in the past, and they build on that that it is eternal in species and temporally originated in individual, and that is a false doctrine and its believer is an unbeliever. And what is found of it with finite individuals, under this division are three divisions: that for which there are no individuals besides those finite individuals, like 'the planets'; and that for which there are individuals besides them and they are infinite, like 'the blessings of Allah Most High'; and that for which there are individuals besides them and they are finite, and that is what the author exemplified by his saying: 'like lion'. In reality, the divisions amount to eight in detail, and by this what some have said here falls, so memorize that. His saying: (and its opposite is the particular), so it is what does not convey sharing, like 'Zaid', for it does not convey sharing. And no heed is paid to what accidental verbal sharing occurs to it, because it has preceded that what is intended here is conceptual sharing. And the author only presented the definition of the universal before the definition of the particular: first, out of concern for it, because it is always the matter of definitions, and mostly of proofs and demands. Al-Anbabi said: His saying 'matter of definitions always and proofs' – he meant by 'definitions' absolute definitions, and by 'proofs' absolute syllogisms, so in his speech there is a predominance. Or the intended meaning is the real definitions and real proofs, so his specification of them is due to their nobility. And his saying: 'and the demands' are the conclusions, because they are sought by evidence. End. (Sabban). Al-Shirshimi: His saying 'Yes, they... etc.' is a corrective, and the blessings of Allah are not like that, for it gives the impression that they are finite even the individuals that do not exist. In some manuscript copies: 'that it' instead of 'from that it'. Al-Shirshimi: His saying 'in the past side' means: and there is no movement but after it a movement in the future side. In the printed edition: 'the planet' instead of 'the planets'. Al-Shirshimi: His saying 'matter of definitions' means: because they are only universal; whether by essential or accidental attributes. And his saying 'mostly' means: because the particular may enter into proofs and demands which are the conclusions; as if he said: 'Zaid is speaking, and every speaker is human' so it yields 'Zaid is human'. And it is because he defined the universal by affirmation and the particular by negation, and affirmation is more honorable than negation. Also, the negation of something is not conceivable except after understanding its existence. And by the first aspect, the precedence of someone other than the author is justified, not by the second aspect, because the other only defined the universal by negation when he said: 'What the very conception of it does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it'. And know that the author's speech is only about the real particular. As for the additive particular, it is: 'what falls under what is more general than it, and between it and the real particular there is absolute generality and specificity, so they come together in 'Zaid' for example, and the additive particular is unique in e.g. 'human'.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the universal (kulli) and the particular (juz'i), along with a discussion of why the author (of al-Sullam) prioritized defining the universal. The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by affirming the matn: 'the universal is what conveys sharing, like lion; and its opposite is the particular.' He then addresses a potential objection: does 'lion' have infinite individuals? He clarifies that infinite individuals are not meant here. He then discusses the division of universals with finite individuals into three types: those with only those finite individuals (e.g., planets), those with additional infinite individuals (e.g., Allah's blessings), and those with additional finite individuals (e.g., lion). He notes that the detailed divisions amount to eight, and this refutes some earlier scholars. He then explains the definition of the particular: it does not convey conceptual sharing, considering only conceptual (ma'nawi) sharing, not verbal (lafzi). He then gives two reasons for prioritizing the universal: first, because it is the matter of definitions and proofs (the author's reason); second, because the universal is defined by affirmation and the particular by negation, and affirmation is more honorable. Al-Anbabi comments that 'definitions' here means absolute definitions and 'proofs' means absolute syllogisms, or real definitions and proofs due to their nobility. Al-Shirshimi adds further clarifications, including a critique of an example. Then al-Bajuri addresses a second reason: the author defined the universal by affirmation, so it is prior; others defined it by negation, so their priority is only justified by the first reason. Finally, he distinguishes between the real particular (juz'i haqiqi) and the additive particular (juz'i idafi): the real particular does not fall under a more general concept, while the additive particular does. They intersect in proper names like Zaid, but the additive particular is unique in common nouns like human. This lesson introduces the fundamental dichotomy of universal and particular, which is essential for understanding definitions and syllogisms.

الجزئي
الاشتراك المعنوي

Key Takeaway
A universal is a concept applicable to multiple individuals; a particular applies to only one, based on meaning not word.

Why the Universal is Defined First
Explains the priority of defining universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two reasons why logicians define the universal before the particular.

Core Idea
The universal is prioritized because it is the material of definitions and proofs, and affirmation is more honorable than negation.

After learning the definitions, we now examine why the author of al-Sullam placed the universal first. The passage gives two reasons: one based on its role in reasoning, another based on the nature of affirmation vs negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first reason: the universal as matter of definitions and proofs
The universal is 'always the matter of definitions' (māddat al-ḥudūd) and 'mostly of proofs and demands'. This means that definitions (ḥudūd) are composed of universals, and proofs (barāhīn) typically involve universal premises. Therefore, it makes sense to define the universal first.
Al-Anbabi clarifies that 'definitions' here means absolute definitions and 'proofs' means absolute syllogisms.
2
Identify the second reason: affirmation vs negation
The author defined the universal by affirmation (ijāb) and the particular by negation (salb). Affirmation is more honorable (ashraf) than negation. Also, negation presupposes existence: you cannot negate something without first understanding it. So the universal, being affirmative, is logically prior.
3
Understand why only the first reason justifies a different order
Some logicians define the universal by negation (e.g., 'what does not prevent sharing'). For them, the second reason (affirmation is more honorable) would not justify priority. Therefore, their priority is justified only by the first reason (the universal is the matter of definitions).
The passage explicitly states this distinction.

فَمُفْهِمُ اشْتِرَاكِ «الكُلّيُّ» كَ: «أَسَدٍ»، وَعَكْسُهُ: «الجُزْئِيُّ». نَعَمْ؛ هِيَ لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا (١) ; بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ مَا مِنْ نِعْمَةٍ إِلَّا وَبَعْدَهَا نِعْمَةٌ ، .. وَهَكَذَا، وَلَيْسَ ذَلِكَ مُرَاداً هُنَا، وَلَا يَصِحُ أَيْضاً التَّمْثِيلُ لِذَلِكَ بِحَرَكَةِ الفَلَكِ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا يَتَمَشَّى إِلَّا عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الفَلَاسِفَةُ مِنْ أَنَّهُ (٢) مَا مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ إِلَّا وَقَبْلَهَا حَرَكَةٌ .. وَهَكَذَا إِلَى مَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهُ فِي جَانِبِ المَاضِي (٣) , وَيَبْنُونَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ أَنَّهَا قَدِيمَةٌ بِالنَّوْعِ حَادِثَةٌ بِالشَّخْصِ , وَهُوَ مَذْهَبٌ بَاطِلٌ، وَمُعْتَقِدُهُ كَافِرٌ. (1) الشرشيمي : قوله : (نَعَمْ ; هِي ... إلخ) استدراك , ونعمة الله تعالى ليست كذلك، فإنَّه يُوهِم أنَّها متناهية حتَّى الأفراد غير الموجودة . (۲) في بعض النسخ الخطية: «بأنه» بدلاً من «من أنه» . (3) الشرشيمي : قوله : ( فِي جَانِبِ المَاضِي أي : وما من حركة إلَّا وبعدها حركة في جانب المستقبل . (٤) في المطبوع: «الكوكب بدلاً من «الكواكب». (0) الشرشيمي : قوله : ( مَادَّةَ الحُدُودِ) أي : لأنَّها لا تكون إلَّا كلِّيَّةٌ ; سواء كانت بالذاتيَّات أو بالعَرَضيَّات. وقوله : (غالباً) أي : لأنَّه قد يدخل الجزئي في البراهين والمطالب التي هي النتائج ; كأن قال : «زَيْدٌ مُتَكَلِّمْ، وَكُلُّ مُتَكَلِّمِ إِنْسَانٌ» فينتج : «زَيْدٌ إِنْسَانٌ» . (٦) انظر : حاشية الصبان على الشرح الصغير للملوي (ص: ٢٥٥). وَلِأَنَّهُ قَدْ عَرَّفَ الكُلِّيَّ بِالإِيجَابِ، وَالجُزْئِيَّ بِالسَّلْبِ، وَالإِيجَابُ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ السَّلْبِ، وَأَيْضاً : سَلْبُ الشَّيْءِ لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ إِلَّا بَعْدَ تَعَقُلِ وُجُودِهِ. وَبِالوَجْهِ الأَوَّلِ يُوَجَّهُ تَقْدِيمُ غَيْرِ المُصَنِّفِ لِذَلِكَ , لَا بِالوَجْهِ الثَّانِي ; لِأَنَّ غَيْرَ الْمُصَنِّفِ إِنَّمَا عَرَّفَ الكُلِّيَّ بِالسَّلْبِ حَيْثُ قَالَ: مَا لَا يَمْنَعُ نَفْسُ تَصَوُّرِهِ مِنْ وُقُوعِ الشَّرِكَةِ فِيهِ (۱) وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ كَلَامَ المُصَنِّفِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الجُزْنِيِّ الحَقِيقِيِّ , وَأَمَّا الجُزْنِيُّ الإِضَافِيُّ فَهُوَ : «مَا انْدَرَجَ تَحْتَ مَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ مِنْهُ , وَبَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الحَقِيقِيّ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ، فَيَجْتَمِعَانِ فِي «زَيْدِ» مَثَلاً، وَيَنْفَرِدُ الإِضَافِيُّ فِي نَحْوِ الإِنْسَانِ».
The universal is what conveys sharing, such as 'lion'; and its opposite is the particular. Yes, they have no limit; meaning: there is no blessing but after it is a blessing, and so on, and that is not intended here. Nor is it correct to exemplify that by the movement of the celestial sphere, because it only applies according to what the philosophers hold: that there is no movement but before it a movement, and so on to infinity in the past, and they build on that that it is eternal in species and temporally originated in individual, and that is a false doctrine and its believer is an unbeliever. And what is found of it with finite individuals, under this division are three divisions: that for which there are no individuals besides those finite individuals, like 'the planets'; and that for which there are individuals besides them and they are infinite, like 'the blessings of Allah Most High'; and that for which there are individuals besides them and they are finite, and that is what the author exemplified by his saying: 'like lion'. In reality, the divisions amount to eight in detail, and by this what some have said here falls, so memorize that. His saying: (and its opposite is the particular), so it is what does not convey sharing, like 'Zaid', for it does not convey sharing. And no heed is paid to what accidental verbal sharing occurs to it, because it has preceded that what is intended here is conceptual sharing. And the author only presented the definition of the universal before the definition of the particular: first, out of concern for it, because it is always the matter of definitions, and mostly of proofs and demands. Al-Anbabi said: His saying 'matter of definitions always and proofs' – he meant by 'definitions' absolute definitions, and by 'proofs' absolute syllogisms, so in his speech there is a predominance. Or the intended meaning is the real definitions and real proofs, so his specification of them is due to their nobility. And his saying: 'and the demands' are the conclusions, because they are sought by evidence. End. (Sabban). Al-Shirshimi: His saying 'Yes, they... etc.' is a corrective, and the blessings of Allah are not like that, for it gives the impression that they are finite even the individuals that do not exist. In some manuscript copies: 'that it' instead of 'from that it'. Al-Shirshimi: His saying 'in the past side' means: and there is no movement but after it a movement in the future side. In the printed edition: 'the planet' instead of 'the planets'. Al-Shirshimi: His saying 'matter of definitions' means: because they are only universal; whether by essential or accidental attributes. And his saying 'mostly' means: because the particular may enter into proofs and demands which are the conclusions; as if he said: 'Zaid is speaking, and every speaker is human' so it yields 'Zaid is human'. And it is because he defined the universal by affirmation and the particular by negation, and affirmation is more honorable than negation. Also, the negation of something is not conceivable except after understanding its existence. And by the first aspect, the precedence of someone other than the author is justified, not by the second aspect, because the other only defined the universal by negation when he said: 'What the very conception of it does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it'. And know that the author's speech is only about the real particular. As for the additive particular, it is: 'what falls under what is more general than it, and between it and the real particular there is absolute generality and specificity, so they come together in 'Zaid' for example, and the additive particular is unique in e.g. 'human'.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the universal (kulli) and the particular (juz'i), along with a discussion of why the author (of al-Sullam) prioritized defining the universal. The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by affirming the matn: 'the universal is what conveys sharing, like lion; and its opposite is the particular.' Real vs Additive Particular
Distinguishes real and additive particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the real particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi) and the additive particular (al-juz'i al-idafi) and give examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A real particular is unique; an additive particular is a universal that is particular in relation to a wider universal.

The passage concludes by introducing a nuanced distinction: not all particulars are the same. The real particular (like 'Zaid') is absolutely unique, while the additive particular (like 'human' when compared to 'animal') is a universal that is particular only in relation to something more general.

Guided Path
1
Define the real particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi)
The real particular is what does not fall under a more general concept. Its meaning applies to only one individual, and it cannot be predicated of many. Example: 'Zaid' refers to a specific person and cannot be said of anyone else in the same sense.
The author's speech about the particular is initially about the real particular.
2
Define the additive particular (al-juz'i al-idafi)
The additive particular is what falls under something more general (a'amm). It is particular only in relation to that wider universal, but it is itself a universal because it can apply to many. Example: 'human' is particular relative to 'animal' because human is a type of animal, but 'human' is universal as it applies to many humans.
The passage says: 'what falls under what is more general than it'.
3
Understand their relationship
The real particular and the additive particular have a relationship of 'general and specific absolutely' (al-umum wa al-khusus bil-itlaq). They intersect in some cases: for example, 'Zaid' is both a real particular (unique) and an additive particular (falls under 'human'). But only the additive particular applies to terms like 'human' which are not real particulars.

Source Text

فَمُفْهِمُ اشْتِرَاكِ «الكُلّيُّ» كَ: «أَسَدٍ»، وَعَكْسُهُ: «الجُزْئِيُّ». نَعَمْ؛ هِيَ لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا (١) ; بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ مَا مِنْ نِعْمَةٍ إِلَّا وَبَعْدَهَا نِعْمَةٌ ، .. وَهَكَذَا، وَلَيْسَ ذَلِكَ مُرَاداً هُنَا، وَلَا يَصِحُ أَيْضاً التَّمْثِيلُ لِذَلِكَ بِحَرَكَةِ الفَلَكِ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا يَتَمَشَّى إِلَّا عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الفَلَاسِفَةُ مِنْ أَنَّهُ (٢) مَا مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ إِلَّا وَقَبْلَهَا حَرَكَةٌ .. وَهَكَذَا إِلَى مَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهُ فِي جَانِبِ المَاضِي (٣) , وَيَبْنُونَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ أَنَّهَا قَدِيمَةٌ بِالنَّوْعِ حَادِثَةٌ بِالشَّخْصِ , وَهُوَ مَذْهَبٌ بَاطِلٌ، وَمُعْتَقِدُهُ كَافِرٌ. (٦) - وَمَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادٌ مُتَنَاهِيَةٌ، وَتَحْتَ هَذَا القِسْمِ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْسَام : - مَا لَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادُ سِوَى تِلْكَ الأَفْرَادِ المُتَنَاهِيَةِ ; كَـ : «الكَوَاكِبِ (٤) - - وَمَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادٌ سِوَاهَا , وَهِيَ غَيْرُ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ ; كَ: نِعْمَةِ اللهِ تَعَالَى». - وَمَا يُوجَدُ لَهُ أَفْرَادٌ سِوَاهَا وَهِيَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٌ، وَهُوَ مَا مَثَّلَ لَهُ المُصَنِّفُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «كَ : أَسَدٍ», فَفِي الحَقِيقَةِ تَؤولُ الأَقْسَامُ إِلَى ثَمَانِيَةٍ تَفْصِيلاً، وَبِهَذَا سَقَط مَا لِبَعْضِهِمْ هُنَا، فَاحْفَظْ ذَلِكَ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَعَكْسُهُ : «الجُزْئِيُّ» فَهُوَ مَا لَا يُفْهِمُ الاشْتِرَاكَ كَـ: «زَيْدِ», فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُفْهِمُ الاشْتِرَاكَ. وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِمَا يَعْرِضُ لَهُ مِنَ الاشْتِرَاكِ اللَّفْظِيّ; لِمَا تَقَدَّمَ مِنْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ هُنَا : الاشْتِرَاكُ المَعْنَوِيُّ. وَإِنَّمَا قَدَّمَ المُصَنِّفُ تَعْرِيفَ الكُلِّيِّ عَلَى تَعْرِيفِ الجُزْئِيِّ : ١ - اهْتِمَاماً بِهِ، لِكَوْنِهِ مَادَّةَ الحُدُودِ دَائِماً , وَالبَرَاهِينِ وَالمَطَالِبِ غَالِباً (٥). الأنبابي قوله : (مَادَّةَ الحُدُودِ دَائِماً , وَالبَرَاهِينِ أراد بـ «الحدود» : مطلق التعاريف، وبـ «البراهين»: مطلق الأقيسة، ففي كلامه تغليب [ب/ ٢٤] . أو المراد : الحدود الحقيقيَّة والبراهين الحقيقية، فيكون تخصيصهما بالذكر; لأشرفيتهما . وقوله : (وَالمَطَالِبِ ) هي النتائج ; لأنَّها تطلب بالدليل . اهـ (صبَّان (٦). (1) الشرشيمي : قوله : (نَعَمْ ; هِي ... إلخ) استدراك , ونعمة الله تعالى ليست كذلك، فإنَّه يُوهِم أنَّها متناهية حتَّى الأفراد غير الموجودة . (۲) في بعض النسخ الخطية: «بأنه» بدلاً من «من أنه» . (3) الشرشيمي : قوله : ( فِي جَانِبِ المَاضِي أي : وما من حركة إلَّا وبعدها حركة في جانب المستقبل . (٤) في المطبوع: «الكوكب بدلاً من «الكواكب». (0) الشرشيمي : قوله : ( مَادَّةَ الحُدُودِ) أي : لأنَّها لا تكون إلَّا كلِّيَّةٌ ; سواء كانت بالذاتيَّات أو بالعَرَضيَّات. وقوله : (غالباً) أي : لأنَّه قد يدخل الجزئي في البراهين والمطالب التي هي النتائج ; كأن قال : «زَيْدٌ مُتَكَلِّمْ، وَكُلُّ مُتَكَلِّمِ إِنْسَانٌ» فينتج : «زَيْدٌ إِنْسَانٌ» . (٦) انظر : حاشية الصبان على الشرح الصغير للملوي (ص: ٢٥٥). وَلِأَنَّهُ قَدْ عَرَّفَ الكُلِّيَّ بِالإِيجَابِ، وَالجُزْئِيَّ بِالسَّلْبِ، وَالإِيجَابُ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ السَّلْبِ، وَأَيْضاً : سَلْبُ الشَّيْءِ لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ إِلَّا بَعْدَ تَعَقُلِ وُجُودِهِ. وَبِالوَجْهِ الأَوَّلِ يُوَجَّهُ تَقْدِيمُ غَيْرِ المُصَنِّفِ لِذَلِكَ , لَا بِالوَجْهِ الثَّانِي ; لِأَنَّ غَيْرَ الْمُصَنِّفِ إِنَّمَا عَرَّفَ الكُلِّيَّ بِالسَّلْبِ حَيْثُ قَالَ: مَا لَا يَمْنَعُ نَفْسُ تَصَوُّرِهِ مِنْ وُقُوعِ الشَّرِكَةِ فِيهِ (۱) وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ كَلَامَ المُصَنِّفِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الجُزْنِيِّ الحَقِيقِيِّ , وَأَمَّا الجُزْنِيُّ الإِضَافِيُّ فَهُوَ : «مَا انْدَرَجَ تَحْتَ مَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ مِنْهُ , وَبَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الحَقِيقِيّ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ، فَيَجْتَمِعَانِ فِي «زَيْدِ» مَثَلاً، وَيَنْفَرِدُ الإِضَافِيُّ فِي نَحْوِ الإِنْسَانِ».
The universal is what conveys sharing, such as 'lion'; and its opposite is the particular. Yes, they have no limit; meaning: there is no blessing but after it is a blessing, and so on, and that is not intended here. Nor is it correct to exemplify that by the movement of the celestial sphere, because it only applies according to what the philosophers hold: that there is no movement but before it a movement, and so on to infinity in the past, and they build on that that it is eternal in species and temporally originated in individual, and that is a false doctrine and its believer is an unbeliever. And what is found of it with finite individuals, under this division are three divisions: that for which there are no individuals besides those finite individuals, like 'the planets'; and that for which there are individuals besides them and they are infinite, like 'the blessings of Allah Most High'; and that for which there are individuals besides them and they are finite, and that is what the author exemplified by his saying: 'like lion'. In reality, the divisions amount to eight in detail, and by this what some have said here falls, so memorize that. His saying: (and its opposite is the particular), so it is what does not convey sharing, like 'Zaid', for it does not convey sharing. And no heed is paid to what accidental verbal sharing occurs to it, because it has preceded that what is intended here is conceptual sharing. And the author only presented the definition of the universal before the definition of the particular: first, out of concern for it, because it is always the matter of definitions, and mostly of proofs and demands. Al-Anbabi said: His saying 'matter of definitions always and proofs' – he meant by 'definitions' absolute definitions, and by 'proofs' absolute syllogisms, so in his speech there is a predominance. Or the intended meaning is the real definitions and real proofs, so his specification of them is due to their nobility. And his saying: 'and the demands' are the conclusions, because they are sought by evidence. End. (Sabban). Al-Shirshimi: His saying 'Yes, they... etc.' is a corrective, and the blessings of Allah are not like that, for it gives the impression that they are finite even the individuals that do not exist. In some manuscript copies: 'that it' instead of 'from that it'. Al-Shirshimi: His saying 'in the past side' means: and there is no movement but after it a movement in the future side. In the printed edition: 'the planet' instead of 'the planets'. Al-Shirshimi: His saying 'matter of definitions' means: because they are only universal; whether by essential or accidental attributes. And his saying 'mostly' means: because the particular may enter into proofs and demands which are the conclusions; as if he said: 'Zaid is speaking, and every speaker is human' so it yields 'Zaid is human'. And it is because he defined the universal by affirmation and the particular by negation, and affirmation is more honorable than negation. Also, the negation of something is not conceivable except after understanding its existence. And by the first aspect, the precedence of someone other than the author is justified, not by the second aspect, because the other only defined the universal by negation when he said: 'What the very conception of it does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it'. And know that the author's speech is only about the real particular. As for the additive particular, it is: 'what falls under what is more general than it, and between it and the real particular there is absolute generality and specificity, so they come together in 'Zaid' for example, and the additive particular is unique in e.g. 'human'.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the universal (kulli) and the particular (juz'i), along with a discussion of why the author (of al-Sullam) prioritized defining the universal. The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by affirming the matn: 'the universal is what conveys sharing, like lion; and its opposite is the particular.' He then addresses a potential objection: does 'lion' have infinite individuals? He clarifies that infinite individuals are not meant here. He then discusses the division of universals with finite individuals into three types: those with only those finite individuals (e.g., planets), those with additional infinite individuals (e.g., Allah's blessings), and those with additional finite individuals (e.g., lion). He notes that the detailed divisions amount to eight, and this refutes some earlier scholars. He then explains the definition of the particular: it does not convey conceptual sharing, considering only conceptual (ma'nawi) sharing, not verbal (lafzi). He then gives two reasons for prioritizing the universal: first, because it is the matter of definitions and proofs (the author's reason); second, because the universal is defined by affirmation and the particular by negation, and affirmation is more honorable. Al-Anbabi comments that 'definitions' here means absolute definitions and 'proofs' means absolute syllogisms, or real definitions and proofs due to their nobility. Al-Shirshimi adds further clarifications, including a critique of an example. Then al-Bajuri addresses a second reason: the author defined the universal by affirmation, so it is prior; others defined it by negation, so their priority is only justified by the first reason. Finally, he distinguishes between the real particular (juz'i haqiqi) and the additive particular (juz'i idafi): the real particular does not fall under a more general concept, while the additive particular does. They intersect in proper names like Zaid, but the additive particular is unique in common nouns like human. This lesson introduces the fundamental dichotomy of universal and particular, which is essential for understanding definitions and syllogisms.

Key Terms
الجزئي الحقيقي
الجزئي الإضافي
العموم والخصوص
