intermediate
7 min
Property (Khassa): Types and Examples
Al-Bajuri explains property, its two types, and the laughter example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between necessary and separable property and explain why laughter is a necessary property of humans.

Core Idea
Property is a non-essential predicable that can be necessary or separable, and it applies to both genus and species with logical dependence.

Moving from genus, al-Bajuri turns to property (khassa), a universal that belongs to all individuals of a species (or genus) but is not part of the essence. He distinguishes two types and discusses a famous example: laughter.

Guided Path
1
Understand that property belongs to both genus and species
Al-Bajuri gives the example of 'walking' as a property of animal, but not necessarily a property of man. This shows dependence.
2
Learn the two types: necessary and separable
Necessary property is inseparable from the species (like potential laughter in humans); separable is not (like actual laughter).
3
Analyze the laughter example
Why do sages consider laughter a necessary property of humans? Because angels and jinn do not laugh by nature; if they do, it's accidental.
4
Respond to an objection
The nisnas (mythical creature) laughs, but that is accidental, not by nature. This defends the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَوَّلُ ثَلَاثَةٌ بِلَا شَطَط: جنس قريبٌ، أَوْ بَعْيدٌ، أَوْ وَسَط 
الباجوري - وَخَرَجَ بِقَوْلِنَا : فِي جَوَابِ : بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الإِضَافَةِ لِـ «أَيُّ» : العَرَضُ العَامُ؛ لِمَا مَرَّ . - وَمَعَ النَّظَرِ لَهَا : الجِنْسُ، وَالنَّوْعُ ؛ لِمَا تَقَدَّمَ . وَبِقَوْلِنَا : فِي عَرَضِهِ» : الفَصْلُ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ فِي جَوَابِ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ هُوَ فِي ذَاتِهِ؟»، كَمَا عَلِمْتَ . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الخَاصَّةَ كَمَا تَكُونُ لِلنَّوْعِ تَكُونُ لِلجِنْسِ ؛ كَـ : المَاشِي، فَإِنَّهُ خَاصَّةٌ لِلحَيَوَانِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَوْنِهَا خَاصَّةٌ لِلجِنْسِ أَنْ تَكُونَ خَاصَّةٌ لِلنَّوْعِ، بِخِلَافِ العَكْسِ، فَكُلُّ خَاصَّةٍ لِلنَّوْعِ خَاصَّةٌ لِلجِنْسِ، وَلَا عَكْسَ. وَالخَاصَّةُ عَلَى نَوْعَيْنِ : (۱) - مُلَازِمَةٌ ؛ كَـ: «الصَّاحِكِ بِالقُوَّةِ». (٢) - وَمُفَارِقَةٌ ؛ كَـ : «الصَّاحِكِ بِالفِعْلِ». وَجَعْلُ الصَّاحِكِ مِنْ خَوَاصُ الإِنْسَانِ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الحُكَمَاءُ مِنْ أَنَّ طَبْعَ المَلَائِكَةِ وَالجِنِّ لَا يَقْتَضِي الصَّحِكَ، كَمَا أَنَّهُ لَا يَقْتَضِي البُكَاءَ ، وَوُقُوعُ ذَلِكَ مِنْهُمْ - كَمَا وَرَدَ فِي بَعْضِ الآثَارِ - اتِّفَاقِيُّ، لَيْسَ بِاقْتِضَاءِ الطَّبْعِ ، وَبِهَذَا يُجَابُ عَمَّا حُكِيَ مِنْ أَنَّ النِّسْنَاسَ يَضْحَكُ إِذَا رَأَى أَوْ سَمِعَ مَا يَتَعَجَّبُ مِنْهُ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ بَعْضُهُمْ مِنْ أَنَّ طَبْعَ المَلَائِكَةِ وَالجِنِّ يَقْتَضِي الضَّحِكَ، فَلَيْسَ «الصَّاحِكُ مِنْ خَوَاصُ الإِنْسَانِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لَهُمَا 
قَوْلُهُ : (وَأَوَّلُ) أي : الَّذِي هُوَ الجِنْسُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (ثَلَاثَةٌ) أَيْ : بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الجِنْسِ المُنْفَرِدِ ؛ لِعَدَمِ الظُّفْرِ بِمِثَالِهِ، وَإِلَّا فَمَعَ النَّظَرِ إِلَيْهِ يَكُونُ الجِنْسُ أَرْبَعَةٌ ، وَمَثَّلَ بَعْضُهُمْ لِلْجِنْسِ المُنْفَرِدِ بـ : «العَقْلِ»؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى جِنْسِيَّتِهِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : ( بِلَا شَطَط) أَيْ : بِلَا زِيَادَةٍ ؛ يَعْنِي: وَلَا نَقْصٍ، فَفِي كَلَامِهِ اكْتِفَاءُ . قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : أَصْلُ قَوْلِهِ : بِلَا شَطَط» : لَا بِشَطَطِ (۱) ؛ لِأَنَّ حَقَّ حَرْفِ النَّفْيِ التَّقْدِيمُ عَلَى جَمِيعِ المَنْفِي (٢) ، وَهُوَ البَاءُ) مَعَ الشَّطَطِ الدَّالُ مَجْمُوعُهُمَا
And the first three without deviation: proximate genus, or remote, or intermediate. Al-Bajuri: And excluded by our saying 'in answer to' while disregarding the relation to 'which?' is the common accident, as has passed. And with the relation to it: genus and species, as has preceded. And by our saying 'in its accident' is the differentia, because it is true in answer to 'what thing is it in its essence?', as you know. And know that property, just as it is for the species, is also for the genus, like 'the walking one', for it is a property for animal. And it is not necessary from its being a property for the genus that it be a property for the species, contrary to the reverse. So every property of the species is a property of the genus, and not vice versa. And property is of two types: (1) necessary, like 'the laughing one potentially'; (2) separable, like 'the laughing one actually'. And making laughter one of the properties of humans is based on what the sages held: that the nature of angels and jinn does not necessitate laughter, just as it does not necessitate weeping. And its occurrence from them, as reported in some traditions, is accidental, not by natural necessity. And with this, one answers what is reported that the nisnas laughs when it sees or hears something that amazes it. But on the view of some that the nature of angels and jinn necessitates laughter, then 'the laughing one' is not among the properties of humans relative to them. His statement: (and the first) meaning that which is the genus. And his statement: (three) meaning by disregarding the separate genus due to inability to find an example of it; otherwise, considering it, genus would be four. And some gave as an example for the separate genus 'the intellect', based on its genus-ness. And his statement: (without deviation) meaning without increase, i.e., also without decrease. So in his statement there is sufficiency. Some said: the origin of his statement 'without deviation' is 'not with deviation', because the particle of negation has the right to precede all that is negated, and it is the 'ba' together with 'deviation' whose indication is their sum.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn stating that the first three (types of genus) are proximate, remote, and intermediate, without excess. Al-Bajuri explains that by our phrase 'in answer to' disregarding relation, the common accident is excluded; with relation, genus and species are included. By 'in its accident', the differentia is excluded because it answers 'in its essence'. He then discusses property: it can belong to both genus and species, with the example of 'walking' for animal. However, a property of genus is not necessarily a property of species. Property is divided into two: necessary (like potential laughter for humans) and separable (like actual laughter). The example of laughter as a property of humans is based on the view that angels and jinn do not naturally laugh; if they do, it is accidental. This answers objections about creatures like the nisnas laughing. Finally, al-Bajuri notes that the matn says 'first three' because the separate genus (like intellect) is excluded due to lack of clear example; without that, genus would be four. The phrase 'without deviation' means without excess or deficiency.

Key Terms
خاصة
خاصة ملازمة
خاصة مفارقة
الصاحك بالقوة
الصاحك بالفعل

C) Potential laughter
D) Weeping

Key Takeaway
Property is a non-essential universal; its necessity depends on whether it is inseparable from the species in nature.

The Special Characteristic (خاصة) and Its Two Types
Author defines special characteristic and its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inseparable and separable special characteristics and determine whether a given property is a special characteristic for a genus or species.

Core Idea
A special characteristic is a non-essential property exclusive to a species or genus.

In Islamic logic, defining terms accurately requires distinguishing essential properties (differentia) from non-essential but exclusive properties (special characteristics). This distinction is vital in Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam, where definitions shape rulings and theological proofs. In this lesson, we explore the definition and types of the special characteristic, using the example of laughter and its application to humans, angels, and jinn as discussed by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic: الخَاصَّة (al-khāṣṣa). This is a universal term that belongs exclusively to a species or genus but is not part of its essence.
It is not a differentia (فصل) because it does not constitute the essence.
2
Extract the genus-species relation
Note that the author states: every special characteristic of a species is also a special characteristic of its genus, but not vice versa. For example, 'walking' is a special characteristic of the genus animal, but not of every species under it.
This highlights the asymmetry: genus properties are broader and may not apply to all species.
3
Distinguish the two types
The author divides special characteristic into inseparable (ملازمة) and separable (مفارقة). Inseparable means the property cannot be removed from the being, like the potential to laugh in humans. Separable means the property can be present or absent, like actual laughter.
The example 'laugher potentially' vs 'laugher actually' clarifies the difference.
4
Apply the philosophical context
The passage discusses that laughter is considered a special characteristic of humans based on the philosophers' view that angels and jinn do not naturally laugh. If their nature did necessitate laughter, then laughter would not be exclusive to humans.
This shows that classification depends on the assumed nature of beings.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Consider the property 'able to speak' for humans. Is it a special characteristic? According to the passage, it would be if it belongs exclusively to humans among known beings. But if animals also speak (e.g., parrots), it might not be exclusive.
This exercise reinforces the condition of exclusivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الخَاصَّةَ كَمَا تَكُونُ لِلنَّوْعِ تَكُونُ لِلجِنْسِ؛ كَـ: المَاشِي، فَإِنَّهُ خَاصَّةٌ لِلحَيَوَانِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَوْنِهَا خَاصَّةٌ لِلجِنْسِ أَنْ تَكُونَ خَاصَّةٌ لِلنَّوْعِ، بِخِلَافِ العَكْسِ، فَكُلُّ خَاصَّةٍ لِلنَّوْعِ خَاصَّةٌ لِلجِنْسِ، وَلَا عَكْسَ. وَالخَاصَّةُ عَلَى نَوْعَيْنِ : (۱) مُلَازِمَةٌ؛ كَـ: «الصَّاحِكِ بِالقُوَّةِ». (٢) وَمُفَارِقَةٌ؛ كَـ : «الصَّاحِكِ بِالفِعْلِ». وَجَعْلُ الصَّاحِكِ مِنْ خَوَاصُ الإِنْسَانِ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الحُكَمَاءُ مِنْ أَنَّ طَبْعَ المَلَائِكَةِ وَالجِنِّ لَا يَقْتَضِي الصَّحِكَ، كَمَا أَنَّهُ لَا يَقْتَضِي البُكَاءَ، وَوُقُوعُ ذَلِكَ مِنْهُمْ - كَمَا وَرَدَ فِي بَعْضِ الآثَارِ - اتِّفَاقِيُّ، لَيْسَ بِاقْتِضَاءِ الطَّبْعِ، وَبِهَذَا يُجَابُ عَمَّا حُكِيَ مِنْ أَنَّ النِّسْنَاسَ يَضْحَكُ إِذَا رَأَى أَوْ سَمِعَ مَا يَتَعَجَّبُ مِنْهُ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ بَعْضُهُمْ مِنْ أَنَّ طَبْعَ المَلَائِكَةِ وَالجِنِّ يَقْتَضِي الضَّحِكَ، فَلَيْسَ «الصَّاحِكُ مِنْ خَوَاصُ الإِنْسَانِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لَهُمَا
And know that the special characteristic (al-khāṣṣa), just as it is for the species, so it is for the genus; like 'the walking one' for it is a special characteristic for animal, and it does not follow from its being a special characteristic for the genus that it be a special characteristic for the species, contrary to the reverse, for every special characteristic for the species is a special characteristic for the genus, and not the opposite. And the special characteristic is of two types: (1) inseparable; like 'the laugher potentially'. (2) separable; like 'the laugher actually'. And making laughter a special characteristic of humans is based on what the philosophers held, that the nature of angels and jinn does not necessitate laughter, just as it does not necessitate weeping, and the occurrence of that from them - as related in some traditions - is accidental, not by necessity of nature, and by this is answered what is narrated that the nasnās laughs when it sees or hears something it wonders at. But on what some held, that the nature of angels and jinn necessitates laughter, then 'the laugher' is not from the special characteristics of humans in relation to them.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines the special characteristic (خاصة) and its division. The author first establishes that a special characteristic can apply both to a species and to its genus. For example, 'walking' is a property of the genus 'animal', but it does not necessarily follow that it is a property of every species under that genus (e.g., fish do not walk). Conversely, any property that belongs to a species (like laughter for humans) also belongs to its genus (animal) in some sense, because the species is under the genus. However, the reverse is not true: a property of the genus may not apply to all species. Then the passage divides the special characteristic into two types: inseparable (ملازمة) and separable (مفارقة). Inseparable means the property cannot be removed from the being, like the potential to laugh in humans. Separable means the property can be present or absent, like actual laughter. The text then discusses the example of laughter as a special characteristic of humans. The philosophers hold that angels and jinn do not have the natural disposition to laugh, so laughter is exclusive to humans. If some hold they do have that nature, then laughter is not a special characteristic of humans. This example illustrates how the classification of a property as a special characteristic depends on the nature of the beings considered.

Key Terms
خاصة
ملازمة
مفارقة

Key Takeaway
A special characteristic is an exclusive non-essential property of a species or genus, and it can be inseparable (potential) or separable (actual).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
In the definition of 'wine' (khamr) in Islamic law, scholars debate whether 'intoxication' is a differentia (essential) or a special characteristic. Using the passage's criteria, analyze which it is and why.

Three Divisions of Genus
Author defines the three divisions of genus: proximate, distant, and intermediate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of genus (proximate, distant, intermediate) and correctly classify given examples.

Core Idea
The classification depends on how many genera are above or below.

This passage introduces the three divisions of genus used in logical definitions. Understanding these divisions is crucial for correctly analyzing definitions in Islamic sciences like Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining criterion
Each division is defined by the presence or absence of a genus above and below it. Proximate has none below, genera above; distant has none above, genera below; intermediate has both above and below.
2
Analyze the example for proximate genus
The text gives 'animal' as an example. Confirm that no genus falls under 'animal' (it is a genus for species like human), and that genera like 'living being' or 'body' are above it.
This is also called the lowest genus.
3
Analyze the examples for distant genus
The unqualified example is 'substance' (jawhar), the highest genus with no genus above. However, when qualified by rank, 'body' can be called distant by one rank, and 'substance' by two ranks.
The qualification by rank allows flexible usage.
4
Analyze the example for intermediate genus
The text gives 'body' as an intermediate genus because it has a genus above (substance) and a genus below (animal). Verify this positioning.
Note that 'body' can also serve as a distant genus when qualified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَوَّلُ ثَلَاثَةٌ بِلَا شَطَط: جِنْسٌ قَرِيبٌ، أَوْ بَعْيدٌ، أَوْ وَسَط. (وَإِنَّمَا قُدِّمَتِ البَاءُ تَزْيِيناً لِلَّفْظِ). وَهَذَا إِنَّمَا يَتَّجِهُ عَلَى القَوْلِ بِأَنَّ لَا فِي مِثْلِ ذَلِكَ لَيْسَتْ بِمَعْنَى: «غَيْر»، وَأَمَّا عَلَى القَوْلِ بِأَنَّهَا بِمَعْنَى: غَيْر كَمَا هُوَ المَشْهُورُ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ: (جِئْتُ بِلَا زَادٍ) فَلَا، فَلْيُعْرَفْ. قَوْلُهُ: (جِنْسٌ قَرِيبٌ) وَيُسَمَّى: الجِنْسَ السَّافِلَ، وَهُوَ مَا لَا جِنْسَ تَحْتَهُ، وَفَوْقَهُ الأَجْنَاسُ؛ كَـ: «الحَيَوَانِ». وَقَوْلُهُ: أَوْ بَعْيدٌ) أَوْ هُنَا وَفِيمَا بَعْدُ بِمَعْنَى: «الوَاوِ». وَيُسَمَّى البَعِيدُ: الجِنْسَ العَالِي وَهُوَ مَا لَا جِنْسَ فَوْقَهُ، وَتَحْتَهُ الأَجْنَاسُ؛ كَـ: «الجَوْهَرِ»، وَهَذَا عِنْدَ الإِطْلَاقِ، وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ التَّفْيِيدِ؛ كَانْ يُقَالَ: بَعِيدٌ بِمَرْتَبَةٍ، أَوْ بِمَرْتَبَتَيْنِ، فَهُوَ بِحَسَبِ القَيْدِ الَّذِي قُيْدَ بِهِ، فَالأَوَّلُ كَـ: (الجِسْمِ)، وَالثَّانِي كَـ: (الجَوْهَرِ). وَقَوْلُهُ: أَوْ وَسَط هُوَ: مَا فَوْقَهُ جِنْسٌ، وَتَحْتَهُ جِنْسٌ؛ كَـ: (الجِسْمِ). (وَإِنَّمَا قَدَّمَ المُصَنِّفُ البَعِيدَ عَلَى الوَسَطِ مَعَ أَنَّ المُعْتَبَرَ فِي تَرْتِيبِ الأَجْنَاسِ النَّصَاعُدُ؛ لأَنَّهُ المُتَيَسِّرُ لَهُ فِي النَّظْم، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى.)
And the first [of the universals] are three without excess: a proximate genus, or a distant one, or an intermediate one. (And the letter bā' was only placed at the beginning for embellishment of the wording.) And this only applies on the view that 'lā' in such phrases does not mean 'other than'; but on the view that it means 'other than' as is well-known in expressions like 'I came without provisions', then no, so let it be known. His statement: 'proximate genus' — and it is called the lowest genus, and it is that which has no genus below it, and above it are genera; like 'animal'. And his statement: 'or distant' — 'or' here and in what follows means 'and'. And the distant is called the highest genus, and it is that which has no genus above it, and below it are genera; like 'substance' — and this is when unqualified; but when qualified, as if it is said 'distant by one rank' or 'by two ranks', then it is according to the restriction with which it is restricted. So the first like 'body', and the second like 'substance'. And his statement: 'or intermediate' — it is that which has a genus above it and a genus below it; like 'body'. (And the author only presented the distant before the intermediate even though the consideration in the arrangement of genera is ascending order, because it was easier for him in the versification, as is not hidden.)
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of a matn line stating that the first of the five universals (the predicables) are three types of genus: proximate, distant, and intermediate. The commentary begins by noting a linguistic point: the letter bā' in 'bilā shaṭaṭ' is placed for embellishment. Then it clarifies that the phrase 'bilā shaṭaṭ' (without excess) relies on interpreting 'lā' as the negative particle, not as 'ghayr' (other than). If 'lā' meant 'other than', the phrase would not convey the intended meaning. The commentator then defines each type of genus: 

- Proximate genus (al-jins al-qarīb, also called the lowest genus) is that which has no genus below it but has genera above it. Example: 'animal' — since animal is a genus for rational/non-rational species, but there are genera above it like 'living being' or 'body'.
- Distant genus (al-jins al-ba‘īd, also called the highest genus) is that which has no genus above it but has genera below it. Example: 'substance' (jawhar) — substance is the highest genus in the category of substance, having no genus above it. However, this is when unqualified; if qualified by distance in ranks, a genus can be called distant relative to another. For instance, 'body' is distant by one rank from species, and 'substance' is distant by two ranks.
- Intermediate genus (al-jins al-wasaṭ) is that which has a genus above it and a genus below it. Example: 'body' — body is a genus for inanimate/animate species, and it is above bodies and below substance.

Finally, the commentator explains why the author presented the distant genus before the intermediate in the versified text (al-sullam): even though the natural order is ascending (proximate → intermediate → distant), the author placed distant before intermediate due to the demands of the poetic meter.

Key Terms
جنس قريب
جنس بعيد
جنس وسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Body, because it has a genus above it (substance) and a genus below it (animal).
D) Plant, because it is a species of living being.

Key Takeaway
The three types of genus (proximate, distant, intermediate) are defined by their position relative to other genera in the hierarchy.

Three Types of Genus: Close, Distant, Middle
Author states three types of genus and commentator explains order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between close, distant, and middle genus, and explain why the author mentions distant before middle in the matn.

Core Idea
The genus is not a single step; it is a hierarchy with close, middle, and distant levels.

We now turn to a subdivision of genus itself. The matn states that the first division of genus comprises three types: close, distant, and middle. At first glance, the order in the matn seems to place 'distant' before 'middle', which goes against the natural ascending order of generality. Al-Bajuri clarifies that this is a poetic convenience, not a logical slip. Let us unpack the three types and the commentator's rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types stated in the matn
Find the terms 'jins qarīb', 'jins baʿīd', 'jins wasaṭ' in the Arabic. Note that the matn says 'three without deviation' – meaning these three are exhaustive and clear-cut.
2
Understand the concept of intermediacy
A close genus has few intermediates between it and the species (e.g., 'animal' for 'human'). A distant genus has many intermediates (e.g., 'substance' for 'human'). A middle genus is in between (e.g., 'body' for 'human'). Visualize a ladder: the species at the bottom, the highest genus at the top, and genera in between.
The number of steps is relative, not absolute.
3
Analyze the commentator's explanation of the order
Al-Bajuri notes that the natural order when arranging genera is ascending (from closer to more distant). Yet the matn has 'distant' before 'middle'. He explains that the author prioritized poetic fluency (nazm) over logical order. Recognize that the poem's meter and rhyme took precedence.
This shows that logical exposition can be adjusted for pedagogical or aesthetic reasons without loss of meaning.
4
Apply the three-type division to a new case
Choose a species from an Islamic science (e.g., 'salat' in fiqh). Determine its close genus (e.g., 'ibadah'), middle genus (e.g., 'action'), and distant genus (e.g., 'thing'). Check if the hierarchy makes sense.
This exercise builds practical familiarity with the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ الْأَلْفَاظِ. (٣٢) وَأَوَّلُ ثَلَاثَةٌ بِلَا شَطَطٍ: جِنْسٌ قَرِيبٌ، أَوْ بَعِيدٌ، أَوْ وَسَطٌ. الْبَاجُورِيُّ: وَإِنَّمَا قَدَّمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ الْبَعِيدَ عَلَى الْوَسَطِ مَعَ أَنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي تَرْتِيبِ الْأَجْنَاسِ النَّصَاعُدُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ الْمُتَيَسِّرُ لَهُ فِي النَّظْمِ، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى.
Section on the investigations of expressions. (32) And the first are three without deviation: a close genus, or a distant one, or a middle one. Al-Bajuri: And indeed the author has placed the distant before the middle, despite the fact that the consideration in the ordering of genera is ascent; because it was easier for him in the poem, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage opens a section on the study of expressions (al-alfāẓ). The matn line states that the first (division of genus) consists of three types without any deviation from the norm: a genus that is close (qarīb) to the species, a genus that is distant (baʿīd), and a genus that is middle (wasaṭ). The close genus is that which has few intermediates between it and the species, such as 'animal' for 'human'. The distant genus has many intermediates, such as 'substance' for 'human'. The middle genus is between them, such as 'body' for 'human'. The commentator, al-Bajuri, then explains why in the matn the author mentioned the distant genus before the middle one, even though the natural order when arranging genera is from the higher (more general) downwards, i.e., ascending in generality. The reason is that the author found it easier to compose the verse in that order, placing 'baʿīd' before 'wasaṭ' for the sake of the meter and rhyme.

Key Terms
جنس قريب
جنس بعيد
جنس وسط
جنس
نوع

D) The commentator mistakenly thought the order was wrong.

Key Takeaway
Genus is threefold relative to a species: close, distant, and middle; deviations from logical order in didactic texts can be due to poetic convenience.

Tawāṭu': Uniformity in Essential Meaning
Author defines tawāṭu' and responds to an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish tawāṭu' from tashākuk by identifying whether variation in individuals occurs in the essential meaning or in external attributes.

Core Idea
Tawāṭu' means equality in essential meaning; variations in perfection do not break it.

This lesson examines the definition of tawāṭu' (univocal) as one of the five relations between word and meaning. It addresses a common objection: if some humans are more perfect than others, doesn't that imply gradation? al-Qarāfī's response clarifies the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term 'tawāṭu'' in the passage and note that it is defined as 'agreement' (توافق).
2
Extract the definition
The definition states that the one meaning applies uniformly to all individuals without difference or variation. The example given is 'human' (insān).
3
Analyze the objection
The objection claims that some humans, like the Prophet ﷺ, have more perfect human qualities, implying gradation (tashākuk). Recognise that this challenges the uniform application.
4
Understand al-Qarāfī's response
Al-Qarāfī explains that the variation is in matters external to the essential meaning (al-musammā). Perfection in attributes does not affect the core meaning, so tawāṭu' remains.
External matters include effects, incompleteness, etc. not part of the definition.
5
Apply the distinction
Test other terms: e.g., 'book' – does it apply equally to all books despite differences in size? Use al-Qarāfī's principle to decide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تواطؤ، تشاكك، تخالف، والاشتراك، عكسه الترادف. قوله: (تواطؤ) أي: توافق، وذلك بأن كان المعنى الواحد مستوياً في أفراده من غير اختلاف وتفاوت فيها؛ كما في الإنسان، فإن معناه لا يختلف في أفراده. فإن قيل: قد يكون المتواطئ في بعض الأفراد أكثر آثاراً وأكمل منه في بعض آخر، وهذا يقتضي أنه متشاكك، وذلك كالإنسان، فإن بعض أفراده كنبينا عليه الصلاة والسلام أكثر وأكمل من غيره في الخواص الإنسانية كالإدراك. أجيب بما قاله القرافي: من أن التفاوت بالأمور الخارجة عن المسمى غير معتبر، حتى يخرج ما ذكر عن التواطؤ. قوله: (تشاكك) أي: بأن يكون المعنى الواحد ليس مستوياً في أفراده، بل مختلف ومتفاوت فيها؛ كما في النور، فإنه في الشمس أقوى منه في غيرها. وإنما سميت هذه النسبة بذلك، لأن الناظر في ذلك يتشكك ويقع في شك، فإنه إن نظر لأصل المعنى كان من قبيل التواطئ، وإلا كان من قبيل الاشتراك، ولذلك أنكر ابن التلمساني حقيقة المشكك حيث قال: لا حقيقة للمشكك؛ لأن ما به التفاوت إن دخل في التسمية فمشترك، وإلا فمتواطئ.
Tawāṭu', tashākuk, takhāluf, and ishtirāk, its opposite is tarāduf. His statement: (tawāṭu') i.e., agreement, and that is when the one meaning is uniform in its individuals without difference or variation in them; as in 'human', for its meaning does not differ in its individuals. If it is said: sometimes the tawāṭu' term in some individuals has more effects and is more perfect than in another individual, and this necessitates that it is tashākuk, and that is like 'human', for some of its individuals like our Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, are more and more perfect than others in human properties like perception. The answer is as al-Qarāfī said: that variation by matters external to the named entity is not considered, such that what was mentioned would remove it from tawāṭu'. His statement: (tashākuk) i.e., that the one meaning is not uniform in its individuals, but rather different and varying in them; as in 'light', for it is stronger in the sun than in other than it. This relation is only called that because the observer in it becomes doubtful and falls into doubt, for if he looks at the origin of the meaning, it is of the type of tawāṭu', and if not, then it is of the type of ishtirāk. For that reason, Ibn al-Tilmisānī denied the reality of the mushakkak, saying: There is no reality for the mushakkak; because that by which the variation occurs, if it enters into the naming, then it is shared, and if not, then it is tawāṭu'.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn line from al-Sullam that lists the five relations. It first defines tawāṭu' as a word whose meaning applies equally to all individuals, giving the example of 'insān' (human). It then addresses an objection: some humans, like the Prophet (peace be upon him), have more perfect human qualities, which seems to imply tashākuk. The response, citing al-Qarāfī, explains that the variation is in attributes external to the essential meaning (al-musammā), so it does not remove the term from tawāṭu'. Next, it defines tashākuk as a word whose meaning is not uniform but varies among individuals, like 'nūr' (light) being stronger in the sun than in other sources. The name 'tashākuk' is explained by the doubt it generates: if one considers the core meaning, it resembles tawāṭu'; if one considers the variation, it resembles ishtirāk. This ambiguity led Ibn al-Tilmisānī to deny the reality of tashākuk, arguing that the differentiating factor either enters the name (making it ishtirāk) or does not (making it tawāṭu').

Key Terms
التواطؤ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Qarāfī, why does the Prophetic perfection not make 'insān' a tashākuk term?
A) Because the Prophet is not human
B) Because the variation is in matters external to the essential meaning
C) Because tashākuk only applies to inanimate objects
D) Because the term 'insān' is actually ambiguous

Key Takeaway
Tawāṭu' is preserved when variation occurs only in accidental attributes, not in the core meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'book' in fiqh: does it apply equally to a handwritten manuscript and a printed copy? Determine if it is tawāṭu' or tashākuk, and justify using the principle of external attributes.

Tashākuk: Gradation and the Doubt It Generates
Author defines tashākuk and explains the doubt, citing Ibn al-Tilmisānī's denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of tashākuk and reconstruct Ibn al-Tilmisānī's argument that tashākuk reduces to either tawāṭu' or ishtirāk.

Core Idea
Tashākuk generates doubt because it sits between univocity and ambiguity.

This lesson explores tashākuk (graded), where meaning varies in intensity. The passage explains why it is called 'doubtful' and presents Ibn al-Tilmisānī's denial that it is a distinct category. His logical argument challenges the reality of tashākuk.

Guided Path
1
Define tashākuk
The passage defines tashākuk as a one meaning that is not uniform but varies among individuals. The example is 'light' (nūr), which is stronger in the sun than in other sources.
2
Understand the doubt
It is called tashākuk (from shakk, doubt) because an observer may be uncertain: if one considers the core meaning, it seems tawāṭu'; if one considers the variation, it seems ishtirāk.
3
Present Ibn al-Tilmisānī's objection
Ibn al-Tilmisānī denied the reality of tashākuk, arguing that any differentiating factor either enters the name (making it ishtirāk) or does not (making it tawāṭu').
4
Formalise the argument
Recognise this as a disjunctive syllogism: either the factor enters the name (ishtirāk) or it does not (tawāṭu'). There is no third option, so tashākuk is not a distinct category.
The disjunction is exclusive and exhaustive.
5
Evaluate the argument
Consider whether the disjunction is truly exhaustive. Could there be a case where the factor partly enters? This leads to debates in later logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Five Term-Meaning Relationships: An Overview
Author enumerates five types of term-meaning relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the five types of term-meaning relationships based on their definitions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
How words relate to meanings: one-to-one, one-to-many, many-to-one, and graded priority.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of word-meaning relationships in Islamic logic. Understanding these categories is essential for precise definition and argumentation in all Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five terms
Find the Arabic names in the first line of the matn: تواطؤ, تشاكك, تخالف, اشتراك, and its opposite ترادف.
Note that ترادف is introduced as 'its opposite'.
2
Understand the core of each
Tawatu' is univocal (equal application). Tashakuk is equivocal by gradation (priority/posteriority). Takhaluf is total difference. Ishtirak is homonymy (one word, many meanings). Taraduf is synonymy (many words, one meaning).
3
Notice the logical relations
Ishtirak and taraduf are opposites. Tashakuk is a distinct intermediate category between univocal and homonymous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
(٣٤) تَوَاطُؤٌ، تَشَاكُكٌ، تَخَالُفٌ، وَالاشْتِرَاكُ، عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ (٣٥) وَاللَّفْظُ إِمَّا طَلَبٌ أَوْ خَبَرُ وَأَوَّلٌ ثَلَاثَةٌ سَتُذكَرُ
الباجوري
وَمَنَعَهُ القَرَّافِيُّ بِمَا مُلَخَصُهُ : أَنَّ المَعْنَى هُنَا وَاحِدٌ، وَهُوَ ( القَدْرُ الشَّامِلُ لِجَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ، فَلَا يَصِحُ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُشْتَرَكِ، وَالتَّفَاوتُ هُنَا بِأُمُورٍ مِنْ جِنْسِ المُسَمَّى، فَلَا يَصِحُ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُتَوَاطِئ، فَتُتْبَتُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ » (٢) ، فَلْيُتَأمَّلْ .
قَوْلُهُ : (تَخَالُفُ أَيْ : تَبَايُنٌ كُلِّيّ ; كَمَا فِي : مَعْنَى الإِنْسَانِ وَمَعْنَى الفَرَسِ»، وَيُمْكِنُ حَمْلُهُ عَلَى مَا يَشْمَلُ : التَّبَايُنَ الجُزْئِيَّ، فَيَدْخُلُ فِيهِ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ، وَالعُمُومُ وَالخُصُوص بِإِطْلَاقِ، كَمَا مَرَّ .
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالاشْتِرَاكُ أَي : اللَّفْظِيُّ ; بِأَنْ يَتَّحِدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَعَدَّدَ مَعْنَاهُ؛ كَمَا فِي عَيْنٍ، فَإِنَّهَا تُطْلَقُ عَلَى البَاصِرَةِ، وَعَلَى الجَارِيَةِ، وَعَلَى الذَّهَبِ، وَعَلَى ذَاتِ الشَّيْءِ (۳)، وَعَلَى خِيَارِ الشَّيْءِ، وَعَلَى الشَّمْسِ، وَعَلَى حَرْفِ الهِجَاءِ المَخْصُوصِ ، وَعَلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ بِالوُقُوفِ عَلَى القَامُوسِ (٤) وَغَيْرِهِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ) أي: التَّتَابِعُ وَالتَّوَارُدُ فِي الاسْتِعْمَالِ عَلَى المَعْنَى الوَاحِدِ; بِأَنْ يَتَعَدَّدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَّحِدَ المَعْنَى ، كَمَا أَشَارَ لَهُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «عَكْسُهُ»؛ كَمَا فِي: «إِنْسَانٍ، وَبَشَرٍ، فَإِنَّهُمَا مُتَتَابِعَانِ وَمُتَوَارِدَانِ عَلَى مَعْنَى وَاحِدٍ، وَهُوَ الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ».
ثُمَّ إِنَّهُ قَدْ يَتَبَادَرُ إِلَى الوَهُم إِعْرَابُ قَوْلِهِ : عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ مُبْتَدَأَ وَخَبَراً، وَهُوَ لَا يُنَاسِبُ إِعْرَابَ قَوْلِهِ : تَوَاطُؤ ... إلخ » بَدَلاً مِنْ خَمْسَةٌ كَمَا فِي نَظَائِرِهِ، فَالأَحْسَنُ أَنْ يُجْعَلَ قَوْلَهُ: «عَكْسُهُ» مَعْطُوفاً عَلَى مَا قَبْلَهُ عَلَى حَذْفِ العَاطِفِ، وَقَوْلَهُ : التَّرَادُفُ بَدَلاً أَوْ عَطْفَ بَيَانِ .
Section on explaining the relationship of words to meanings. (34) Tawatu' (univocal), tashakuk (equivocal by gradation), takhaluf (difference), and ishtirak (homonymy); its opposite is taraduf (synonymy) (35). And the word is either a request or a report, and the first has three types that will be mentioned. Al-Bajuri: Al-Qarafi refuted it with a summary: the meaning here is one, and it is the common notion covering all individuals, so it is not valid for it to be of the type of homonymy; the variation here is by matters of the same kind as the named entity, so it is not valid for it to be of the type of univocal; thus a reality is established for it. So reflect. His statement: (takhaluf) i.e., total difference, as in the meaning of human and the meaning of horse. And it is possible to interpret it to include partial difference, so it includes generality and specificity in some respects and absolute generality and specificity, as previously mentioned. His statement: (al-ishitirak) i.e., verbal homonymy, that the word is one and its meanings are multiple, as in 'ayn, for it is applied to the eyeball, to a flowing spring, to gold, to the essence of a thing, to the best part of a thing, to the sun, to a specific letter of the alphabet, and to other things as known by consulting the Qamus and other works. His statement: (its opposite is taraduf) i.e., successive occurrence and concurrence in usage upon a single meaning, with multiple words and one meaning, as he indicated by his saying 'its opposite'; as in 'insan' and 'bashar', for they are successive and concur upon one meaning, which is rational animal. Then it may occur to the mind to parse his statement 'its opposite is taraduf' as a subject and predicate, but that does not suit the parsing of his statement 'tawatu'... etc.' as a substitute for 'five' as in similar constructions. So it is better to treat his statement 'its opposite' as coordinated to what precedes without the conjunction, and his statement 'taraduf' as a substitute or explanatory apposition.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage defines five key relationships between a word and its meaning, foundational for logic. The author first enumerates them: tawatu' (univocal), where the word applies equally to all its referents (e.g., 'animal' applies equally to human and horse); tashakuk (equivocal by gradation), where the meaning is one but differentiated by priority and posteriority (e.g., 'existence' is said of God primarily and of creatures derivatively); takhaluf (difference), where words have completely distinct meanings, like 'human' and 'horse'; ishtirak (homonymy), where one word has multiple unrelated meanings, like 'ayn' (eye, spring, gold, etc.); taraduf (synonymy), where multiple words share one meaning, like 'insan' and 'bashar' both meaning rational animal. Al-Bajuri then comments on each. He records al-Qarafi's objection regarding tashakuk: al-Qarafi argued that the meaning in tashakuk is one (the common notion covering all individuals) and the variation is by matters intrinsic to the named entity, so it is neither homonymous nor univocal, but a distinct category—this is a nuanced point. For takhaluf, the author clarifies it as total difference, but notes it can be extended to partial difference, which includes generic and specific relations. For ishtirak, he emphasizes it is verbal homonymy (not conceptual) and gives the classic example of 'ayn' with eight meanings. For taraduf, he defines it as multiple words for one meaning and explains the syntax of the matn: 'its opposite is taraduf' should not be read as a new sentence but as a coordinated phrase to maintain consistency with the earlier enumeration of five terms. This careful parsing shows the author's precision.

Key Terms
تواطؤ (tawāṭuʾ): A word applied to multiple individuals equally, without priority or disparity (univocal). [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
تشاكك (tashākuk): A word applied to multiple individuals with priority and posteriority, by gradation (equivocal by analogy/proportion). [Note: Some later logicians subsume this under 'mushtarak' but with qualification; here it is a distinct category.]
تخالف (takhāluf): Words with completely different meanings (total difference), can also include partial difference like generality and specificity. [Note: Stable as the opposite of agreement.]
اشتراك (ishtirāk): One word with multiple unrelated meanings (verbal homonymy). [Note: Often contrasted with 'tawāṭuʾ' and 'tashākuk'; stable.]
ترادف (tarāduf): Multiple words with one meaning (synonymy). [Note: Stable; some debate about absolute synonymy in Arabic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs correctly identifies ishtirak and taraduf?
A) Ishtirak: one word, many meanings; Taraduf: many words, one meaning.
B) Ishtirak: many words, one meaning; Taraduf: one word, many meanings.
C) Ishtirak: priority and posteriority; Taraduf: total difference.
D) Ishtirak: univocal; Taraduf: homonymy.

Key Takeaway
The five categories immediately reveal the logical structure behind lexical relationships, a cornerstone of precise reasoning in Islamic thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the five types from memory and give a one-word Islamic example for each (e.g., 'salat' for tawatu', 'wajib' for tashakuk).

The Five Universals

Tawatu' (Univocal) and Tashakuk (Graded Equivocal)
Author defines tawatu' and tashakuk and records al-Qarafi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish tawatu' from tashakuk and explain al-Qarafi's objection regarding tashakuk's category.

Core Idea
Tawatu' is equal sharing; tashakuk is graded sharing—a debated intermediate category.

Among the five relationships, tawatu' and tashakuk both involve a single meaning shared among multiple entities, but with a crucial difference: in tawatu' the sharing is equal, whereas in tashakuk it is by priority and posteriority. This subtlety sparked discussion, as recorded in al-Qarafi's objection.

Guided Path
1
Recall definitions
Tawatu': a word applies equally to all individuals (e.g., 'animal' to human and horse). Tashakuk: a word applies with priority and posteriority (e.g., 'existence' to God first, then creatures).
2
Locate al-Qarafi's objection in the commentary
Al-Bajuri reports that al-Qarafi argued tashakuk is neither ishtirak (because meaning is one) nor tawatu' (because variation is by matters of the same kind as the named entity).
3
Understand the logical consequence
This objection suggests a third distinct category: a word denoting a single meaning but with intrinsic gradation.
4
Consider theological application
Terms like 'wujud' (existence) are commonly understood as tashakuk.

Source Text

وَمَنَعَهُ القَرَّافِيُّ بِمَا مُلَخَصُهُ : أَنَّ المَعْنَى هُنَا وَاحِدٌ، وَهُوَ ( القَدْرُ الشَّامِلُ لِجَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ، فَلَا يَصِحُ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُشْتَرَكِ، وَالتَّفَاوتُ هُنَا بِأُمُورٍ مِنْ جِنْسِ المُسَمَّى، فَلَا يَصِحُ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُتَوَاطِئ، فَتُتْبَتُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ » (٢) ، فَلْيُتَأمَّلْ .
قَوْلُهُ : (تَخَالُفُ أَيْ : تَبَايُنٌ كُلِّيّ ; كَمَا فِي : مَعْنَى الإِنْسَانِ وَمَعْنَى الفَرَسِ»، وَيُمْكِنُ حَمْلُهُ عَلَى مَا يَشْمَلُ : التَّبَايُنَ الجُزْئِيَّ، فَيَدْخُلُ فِيهِ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ، وَالعُمُومُ وَالخُصُوص بِإِطْلَاقِ، كَمَا مَرَّ .
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالاشْتِرَاكُ أَي : اللَّفْظِيُّ ; بِأَنْ يَتَّحِدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَعَدَّدَ مَعْنَاهُ؛ كَمَا فِي عَيْنٍ، فَإِنَّهَا تُطْلَقُ عَلَى البَاصِرَةِ، وَعَلَى الجَارِيَةِ، وَعَلَى الذَّهَبِ، وَعَلَى ذَاتِ الشَّيْءِ (۳)، وَعَلَى خِيَارِ الشَّيْءِ، وَعَلَى الشَّمْسِ، وَعَلَى حَرْفِ الهِجَاءِ المَخْصُوصِ ، وَعَلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ بِالوُقُوفِ عَلَى القَامُوسِ (٤) وَغَيْرِهِ.

ثُمَّ إِنَّهُ قَدْ يَتَبَادَرُ إِلَى الوَهُم إِعْرَابُ قَوْلِهِ : عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ مُبْتَدَأَ وَخَبَراً، وَهُوَ لَا يُنَاسِبُ إِعْرَابَ قَوْلِهِ : تَوَاطُؤ ... إلخ » بَدَلاً مِنْ خَمْسَةٌ كَمَا فِي نَظَائِرِهِ، فَالأَحْسَنُ أَنْ يُجْعَلَ قَوْلَهُ: «عَكْسُهُ» مَعْطُوفاً عَلَى مَا قَبْلَهُ عَلَى حَذْفِ العَاطِفِ، وَقَوْلَهُ : التَّرَادُفُ بَدَلاً أَوْ عَطْفَ بَيَانِ .

Takhaluf (Difference) and Ishtirak (Homonymy)
Author defines takhaluf and ishtirak with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between takhaluf (total difference) and ishtirak (verbal homonymy) and identify examples in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Takhaluf is two words with different meanings; ishtirak is one word with different meanings—both involve difference, but at different levels.

This lesson covers two relationships that involve diversity: takhaluf, where two words have completely different meanings, and ishtirak, where one word has multiple unrelated meanings. Understanding them avoids confusion in interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Define takhaluf
Takhaluf is total difference between two words, e.g., 'insan' and 'faras'. The passage notes it can include partial difference like generality and specificity.
2
Define ishtirak
Ishtirak is homonymy: one word with multiple unrelated meanings. Example: 'ayn' with eight meanings listed.
3
Compare
Takhaluf involves two different words; ishtirak involves one word. Both involve difference in meaning.
4
Recognize subcategories
Takhaluf can be total or partial; ishtirak is about multiple distinct meanings for one word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
(٣٤) تَوَاطُؤٌ، تَشَاكُكٌ، تَخَالُفٌ، وَالاشْتِرَاكُ، عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ (٣٥) وَاللَّفْظُ إِمَّا طَلَبٌ أَوْ خَبَرُ وَأَوَّلٌ ثَلَاثَةٌ سَتُذكَرُ
الباجوري
وَمَنَعَهُ القَرَّافِيُّ بِمَا مُلَخَصُهُ : أَنَّ المَعْنَى هُنَا وَاحِدٌ، وَهُوَ ( القَدْرُ الشَّامِلُ لِجَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ، فَلَا يَصِحُ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُشْتَرَكِ، وَالتَّفَاوتُ هُنَا بِأُمُورٍ مِنْ جِنْسِ المُسَمَّى، فَلَا يَصِحُ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُتَوَاطِئ، فَتُتْبَتُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ » (٢) ، فَلْيُتَأمَّلْ .
قَوْلُهُ : (تَخَالُفُ أَيْ : تَبَايُنٌ كُلِّيّ ; كَمَا فِي : مَعْنَى الإِنْسَانِ وَمَعْنَى الفَرَسِ»، وَيُمْكِنُ حَمْلُهُ عَلَى مَا يَشْمَلُ : التَّبَايُنَ الجُزْئِيَّ، فَيَدْخُلُ فِيهِ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ، وَالعُمُومُ وَالخُصُوص بِإِطْلَاقِ، كَمَا مَرَّ .
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالاشْتِرَاكُ أَي : اللَّفْظِيُّ ; بِأَنْ يَتَّحِدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَعَدَّدَ مَعْنَاهُ؛ كَمَا فِي عَيْنٍ، فَإِنَّهَا تُطْلَقُ عَلَى البَاصِرَةِ، وَعَلَى الجَارِيَةِ، وَعَلَى الذَّهَبِ، وَعَلَى ذَاتِ الشَّيْءِ (۳)، وَعَلَى خِيَارِ الشَّيْءِ، وَعَلَى الشَّمْسِ، وَعَلَى حَرْفِ الهِجَاءِ المَخْصُوصِ ، وَعَلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ بِالوُقُوفِ عَلَى القَامُوسِ (٤) وَغَيْرِهِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ) أي: التَّتَابِعُ وَالتَّوَارُدُ فِي الاسْتِعْمَالِ عَلَى المَعْنَى الوَاحِدِ; بِأَنْ يَتَعَدَّدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَّحِدَ المَعْنَى ، كَمَا أَشَارَ لَهُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «عَكْسُهُ»؛ كَمَا فِي: «إِنْسَانٍ، وَبَشَرٍ، فَإِنَّهُمَا مُتَتَابِعَانِ وَمُتَوَارِدَانِ عَلَى مَعْنَى وَاحِدٍ، وَهُوَ الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ».
ثُمَّ إِنَّهُ قَدْ يَتَبَادَرُ إِلَى الوَهُم إِعْرَابُ قَوْلِهِ : عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ مُبْتَدَأَ وَخَبَراً، وَهُوَ لَا يُنَاسِبُ إِعْرَابَ قَوْلِهِ : تَوَاطُؤ ... إلخ » بَدَلاً مِنْ خَمْسَةٌ كَمَا فِي نَظَائِرِهِ، فَالأَحْسَنُ أَنْ يُجْعَلَ قَوْلَهُ: «عَكْسُهُ» مَعْطُوفاً عَلَى مَا قَبْلَهُ عَلَى حَذْفِ العَاطِفِ، وَقَوْلَهُ : التَّرَادُفُ بَدَلاً أَوْ عَطْفَ بَيَانِ .
Section on explaining the relationship of words to meanings. (34) Tawatu' (univocal), tashakuk (equivocal by gradation), takhaluf (difference), and ishtirak (homonymy); its opposite is taraduf (synonymy) (35). And the word is either a request or a report, and the first has three types that will be mentioned. Al-Bajuri: Al-Qarafi refuted it with a summary: the meaning here is one, and it is the common notion covering all individuals, so it is not valid for it to be of the type of homonymy; the variation here is by matters of the same kind as the named entity, so it is not valid for it to be of the type of univocal; thus a reality is established for it. So reflect. His statement: (takhaluf) i.e., total difference, as in the meaning of human and the meaning of horse. And it is possible to interpret it to include partial difference, so it includes generality and specificity in some respects and absolute generality and specificity, as previously mentioned. His statement: (al-ishitirak) i.e., verbal homonymy, that the word is one and its meanings are multiple, as in 'ayn, for it is applied to the eyeball, to a flowing spring, to gold, to the essence of a thing, to the best part of a thing, to the sun, to a specific letter of the alphabet, and to other things as known by consulting the Qamus and other works. His statement: (its opposite is taraduf) i.e., successive occurrence and concurrence in usage upon a single meaning, with multiple words and one meaning, as he indicated by his saying 'its opposite'; as in 'insan' and 'bashar', for they are successive and concur upon one meaning, which is rational animal. Then it may occur to the mind to parse his statement 'its opposite is taraduf' as a subject and predicate, but that does not suit the parsing of his statement 'tawatu'... etc.' as a substitute for 'five' as in similar constructions. So it is better to treat his statement 'its opposite' as coordinated to what precedes without the conjunction, and his statement 'taraduf' as a substitute or explanatory apposition.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage defines five key relationships between a word and its meaning, foundational for logic. The author first enumerates them: tawatu' (univocal), where the word applies equally to all its referents (e.g., 'animal' applies equally to human and horse); tashakuk (equivocal by gradation), where the meaning is one but differentiated by priority and posteriority (e.g., 'existence' is said of God primarily and of creatures derivatively); takhaluf (difference), where words have completely distinct meanings, like 'human' and 'horse'; ishtirak (homonymy), where one word has multiple unrelated meanings, like 'ayn' (eye, spring, gold, etc.); taraduf (synonymy), where multiple words share one meaning, like 'insan' and 'bashar' both meaning rational animal. Al-Bajuri then comments on each. He records al-Qarafi's objection regarding tashakuk: al-Qarafi argued that the meaning in tashakuk is one (the common notion covering all individuals) and the variation is by matters intrinsic to the named entity, so it is neither homonymous nor univocal, but a distinct category—this is a nuanced point. For takhaluf, the author clarifies it as total difference, but notes it can be extended to partial difference, which includes generic and specific relations. For ishtirak, he emphasizes it is verbal homonymy (not conceptual) and gives the classic example of 'ayn' with eight meanings. For taraduf, he defines it as multiple words for one meaning and explains the syntax of the matn: 'its opposite is taraduf' should not be read as a new sentence but as a coordinated phrase to maintain consistency with the earlier enumeration of five terms. This careful parsing shows the author's precision.

Key Terms
تواطؤ (tawāṭuʾ): A word applied to multiple individuals equally, without priority or disparity (univocal). [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
تشاكك (tashākuk): A word applied to multiple individuals with priority and posteriority, by gradation (equivocal by analogy/proportion). [Note: Some later logicians subsume this under 'mushtarak' but with qualification; here it is a distinct category.]
تخالف (takhāluf): Words with completely different meanings (total difference), can also include partial difference like generality and specificity. [Note: Stable as the opposite of agreement.]
اشتراك (ishtirāk): One word with multiple unrelated meanings (verbal homonymy). [Note: Often contrasted with 'tawāṭuʾ' and 'tashākuk'; stable.]
ترادف (tarāduf): Multiple words with one meaning (synonymy). [Note: Stable; some debate about absolute synonymy in Arabic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The word 'ayn' is given as an example of which type?
A) tawatu'
B) tashakuk
C) ishtirak
D) taraduf

Key Takeaway
Takhaluf and ishtirak both involve difference, but takhaluf is about different words with different meanings, while ishtirak is about one word with multiple meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether 'kitab' and 'qalam' are an example of takhaluf or ishtirak, and explain.

Taraduf (Synonymy) and the Parsing of the Matn
Author defines taraduf and gives a grammatical note on parsing the matn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define taraduf, give its example, and explain the author's recommended parsing of 'its opposite is taraduf'.

Core Idea
Taraduf is the opposite of ishtirak: many words, one meaning; correct grammatical parsing preserves the list's structure.

Taraduf, or synonymy, is the relationship where multiple words share a single meaning. The passage gives the example of 'insan' and 'bashar'. Additionally, the author comments on the proper grammatical parsing of the matn to maintain the list structure, ensuring logical precision.

Guided Path
1
Define taraduf
Taraduf is when multiple words have one meaning. Example: 'insan' and 'bashar' both mean rational animal.
2
Identify the grammatical issue
The phrase 'its opposite is taraduf' could be read as a new sentence (subject-predicate), but that would break the list structure of the matn.
3
Follow the author's parsing
The author recommends treating 'its opposite' as coordinated to the previous items, and 'taraduf' as a substitute or explanatory apposition. This keeps the list coherent.
4
Appreciate the link
The grammatical trick reinforces the logical relationship: ishtirak and taraduf are opposites, and the matn presents them as a single pair within the list.

Distinguishing Aggregate from Distributive Universal
Al-Bajuri defines 'al-kull' and analyses a hadith to illustrate ambiguity between two types of universal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a judgment on the aggregate (hukm 'ala al-majmu') and a distributive universal judgment (kulliyya), and apply this distinction to interpret a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
A universal can mean either the whole group or each individual — context determines the truth.

In this passage from al-Bajuri's commentary, we encounter a crucial logical distinction: the difference between judging something as a collective whole versus judging each member individually. This distinction is not just theoretical; it is necessary for correctly understanding statements in fiqh and kalam, as shown by the analysis of a hadith where the Prophet's response to a question about prayer could be interpreted either way. We will walk through the definition and the logical reasoning behind the preferred interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definition of 'al-kull' provided by al-Bajuri. It is defined as 'our judgment upon the aggregate' (hukmuna 'ala al-majmu). Note that this is a judgment on the whole group collectively.
Compare this with the standard logical term 'kulliyya' which applies to each individual.
2
Contrast with distributive universal
Recognize that 'al-kull' here refers to aggregate judgment, while 'kulliyya' refers to distributive judgment. The example of Banu Tamim shows ambiguity: the statement could mean all together (aggregate) or some together (figuratively).
The passage states that using 'aggregate' to mean 'some' is figurative (majaz).
3
Examine the hadith's two interpretations
The Prophet said 'All of that did not happen' in response to a question about prayer shortening and forgetfulness. Two interpretations: (a) judgment on aggregate – denying both collectively; (b) distributive universal – denying each individually.
The author initially leans toward aggregate because forgetfulness did occur, but that would make the statement false if distributive.
4
Evaluate the verifying scholars' argument
The preponderant view is distributive universal. Reason: Dhu al-Yadayn replied 'Rather, some of that did happen'. A particular affirmative contradicts a universal negative but not an aggregate judgment. Hence, the Prophet's statement must be universal negative.
This shows how logical form determines correct interpretation.
5
Apply the principle to other statements
Practice distinguishing the two types in other Islamic texts, such as 'All Muslims are brothers' or 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Decide whether each is aggregate or distributive.

Source Text

فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
(٣٧) «الكُلُّ : حُكْمُنَا عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ كَقَوْلِهِ: «كُلُّ ذَاكَ لَيْسَ ذَا وُقُوعِ»
وَقَدْ يَكُونُ الكَلَامُ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلأَمْرَيْنِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِمْ: بَنُو تَمِيمٍ يَحْمِلُونَ الصَّخْرَةَ العَظِيمَةَ، فَإِنَّهُ يَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ المُرَادُ مَجْمُوعَ جَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ؛ لِكَوْنِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمْ لَا يَسْتَقِلُّ بِالحَمْلِ، وَأَنْ يَكُونَ المُرَادُ مَجْمُوعَ بَعْضِهَا؛ لِكَوْنِهِ يَسْتَقِلُّ بِهِ. وَبِمَا تَقَرَّرَ يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ: إِنَّ المَجْمُوعَ قَدْ يُرَادُ بِهِ البَعْضُ، مَحْمُولٌ عَلَى أَنَّ ذَلِكَ عَلَى طَرِيقِ المَجَازِ.
قَوْلُهُ: (كَـ : كُلِّ ... إلخ) هَذِهِ رِوَايَةٌ بِالمَعْنَى، وَإِلَّا فَالمَرْوِيُّ: أَنَّهُ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَكُنْ. وَاسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ عَائِدٌ لِلمَذْكُورِ مِنْ قَصْرِ الصَّلَاةِ وَالنِّسْيَانِ فِي قَوْلِ ذِي اليَدَيْنِ لَمَّا سَلَّمَ صلى الله عليه وسلم مِنْ رَكْعَتَيْنِ: أَقَصُرَتِ الصَّلَاةُ أَمْ نَسِيتَ يَا رَسُولَ اللهِ؟ وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الحَدِيثُ المَذْكُورُ مِنْ بَابِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ المَنْفِيُّ فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ لِثْبُوتِ أَحَدِهِمَا - وَهُوَ النِّسْيَانُ ـ فِيهِ، فَلَوْ كَانَ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ لَكَانَ الخَبَرُ غَيْرَ مُوَافِقٍ لِلوَاقِعِ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ لَائِقٍ بِهِ. هَذَا تَوْجِيهُ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ، وَالرَّاجِحُ عِنْدَ المُحَقِّقِينَ أَنَّهُ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، وَمُخَالَفَةُ الخَبَرِ لِلوَاقِعِ إِنَّمَا تُعَدُّ عَيْباً إِذَا عَلِمَهَا المُخْبَرُ، وَيَشْهَدُ لِهَذَا مَا رُوِيَ فِي بَعْضِ الطُّرُقِ: «لَمْ أَنْسَ وَلَمْ نَقْصُرْ»، وَمَا رُوِيَ مِنْ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا قَالَ صلى الله عليه وسلم ذَلِكَ قَالَ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ: بَلْ بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ، فَلَوْ لَمْ يَكُنِ الحَدِيثُ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ لَمَا صَحَّ قَوْلُهُ المَذْكُورُ؛ لأنَّ الإِيجَابَ الجُزْئِيَّ إِنَّمَا يَرْفَعُ السَّلْبَ الكُلِّيَّ.
Chapter on explaining the relation of words to meanings. (37) Al-kull: our judgment upon the aggregate, like his saying: 'All of that did not occur.' And sometimes speech is ambiguous between the two, as in their saying: 'The Banu Tamim carry the great rock.' For it may be that the intended meaning is the aggregate of all individuals, because each of them cannot independently carry it, or the intended meaning may be the aggregate of some of them, because they can independently carry it. And from what is established it is known that their saying: 'The aggregate may be used to mean some of it' is carried on that it is figurative. His saying: (like: 'all' etc.) this is a narration by meaning, otherwise the narrated hadith is that he (peace be upon him) said: 'All of that did not happen.' And the demonstrative pronoun refers to what was mentioned of the shortening of the prayer and forgetfulness in the statement of Dhu al-Yadayn when he (peace be upon him) gave salam after two rak'ahs: 'Was the prayer shortened or did you forget, O Messenger of Allah?' And indeed the aforementioned hadith is from the category of judgment on the aggregate because that which is denied in reality is due to the establishment of one of them - namely forgetfulness - in it. Had it been from the category of the universal, the report would not correspond to reality, which is not befitting of him. This is the direction of the author's statement. And the preponderant view according to the verifying scholars is that it is from the category of the universal. And the report's discrepancy with reality is only considered a defect if the informant knows it. And supporting this is what is narrated in some versions: 'I did not forget and I did not shorten,' and what is narrated that when he (peace be upon him) said that, Dhu al-Yadayn said: 'Rather, some of that did happen.' For if the hadith were not from the category of the universal, his statement would not be correct, because the particular affirmative only negates the universal negative.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytic lesson from al-Bajuri's commentary. It begins by defining 'al-kull' (the universal) as 'our judgment upon the aggregate' (hukmuna 'ala al-majmu'), giving the example of the Prophet's statement 'All of that did not occur' from a hadith. The author then explains that some expressions can be ambiguous between two interpretations: a judgment on the entire group collectively, or a judgment on a subset of the group distributively. He illustrates this with the example 'Banu Tamim carry the great rock'—this could mean either all of them together carry it (none alone can), or some of them carry it (because they individually can). This distinction is crucial for understanding how universal propositions function. The author further clarifies that when the aggregate (majmu') is used to indicate a part (ba'd), it is a figurative usage (majaz). He then applies this analysis to the hadith where the Prophet was asked after praying two rak'ahs: 'Was the prayer shortened or did you forget?' The Prophet replied, 'All of that did not happen.' Some argued this was a judgment on the aggregate (denying both collectively), because forgetfulness did occur (the Prophet did forget due to revelation). But the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) hold that it is actually a universal judgment (kulliyya) negating each individually. They argue that a report contradicting reality is only a defect if the speaker knows it to be false; the Prophet spoke according to what he believed at the moment. Moreover, in some narrations the Prophet specifically said 'I did not forget and I did not shorten' (universal negative of each), and Dhu al-Yadayn responded 'Rather, some of that did happen'—this response only makes sense if the Prophet's statement was a universal negation, because a particular affirmative ('some of that') contradicts a universal negative but not a judgment on the aggregate (which can be true even if part is affirmed). Thus, the passage teaches how to identify and resolve ambiguity in universal propositions, using a fiqh example from the Prophet's life.

Key Terms
الكُلُّ
المَجْمُوع
الكُلِّيَّة
الحُكْمِ عَلَى المَجْمُوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hadith 'All of that did not happen' (about shortening and forgetfulness), which interpretation do the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) consider correct?
A) It is a judgment on the aggregate (hukm 'ala al-majmu'), denying both collectively.
B) It is a universal judgment (kulliyya), denying each individually.
C) It is ambiguous and cannot be decided.
D) It is a particular negative, denying only one of the two.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between judgment on aggregate and distributive universal is essential for correctly interpreting universal statements, especially in prophetic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyse the statement 'Banu Tamim carry the great rock' using the two interpretations (aggregate of all vs. aggregate of some). Determine which interpretation is correct if each individual can or cannot carry the rock alone.
?
Given a statement like 'All of the companions are trustworthy', discuss whether it should be interpreted as a judgment on the aggregate (the group as a whole is trustworthy) or distributive (each companion is trustworthy). What implications does each interpretation have for the reliability of individual companions?

Distinguishing Antecedent and Consequent in Conditionals
Author defines muqaddam and tali for muttasila and argues munfasila lacks them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muttasila and munfasila by identifying whether a conditional proposition has a muqaddam and tali, and explain why munfasila lacks them.

Core Idea
In muttasila, order matters; in munfasila, it does not.

In this lesson, we examine the technical definitions of the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in conditional propositions. You will learn how logicians define these terms specifically for conjunctive conditionals (muttasila) and why disjunctive conditionals (munfasila) have no such fixed parts. This distinction is crucial for correctly parsing conditional arguments in Islamic logic, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Recognize that there are two main types: muttasila (if-then) and munfasila (either-or). In muttasila, the order of parts is meaningful; in munfasila, it is not.
2
Extract the definition of muqaddam and tali
According to Ibn Ya'qub, in muttasila, the muqaddam is the part entered by the conditional particle (the antecedent), and the tali is what is attached to it (the consequent).
This definition is standard in Arabic logic.
3
Test on a muttasila example
Consider: 'If the sun rises, then it is day.' The part after 'if' is muqaddam; the rest is tali. Reversing changes the meaning, confirming the fixed order.
Use Islamic example: 'If the evidence is definitive, then the ruling is certain.'
4
Test on a munfasila example
Consider: 'Either it is a human or it is not a human.' Reversing to 'Either it is not a human or it is a human' does not change the meaning. Hence, no fixed muqaddam or tali.
Use Islamic example: 'Either this action is permissible or it is forbidden.'
5
Address the counterargument about meaningful order
Some argue that if one part affirms and the other negates, there is a meaningful order because negation presupposes affirmation. The standard response is that this order is only in mention, not in the opposition (al-'inād). Alternatively, the necessary meaningful order is denied in every munfasila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥٩) جُزَآهُمَا : مُقَدَّمُ، وَتَالِي أَمَّا بَيَانُ ذَاتِ الاتّصَالِ : الباجوري وَقَدْ صَرَّحَ بِهِ أَيْضاً ابْنُ يَعْقُوبَ حَيْثُ قَالَ: المَشْهُورُ فِي الاِصْطِلَاحِ أَنَّ المُقَدَّمَ هُوَ مَدْخُولُ أَدَاةِ الشَّرْطِ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، وَالتَّالِي مَا عُلِّقَ عَلَى مَدْخُولِهَا ، وَأَمَّا المُنْفَصِلَةُ فَلَا مُقَدَّمَ لَهَا وَلَا تَالِي؛ لأَنَّ المَعْنَى لَا يَخْتَلِفُ فِيهَا بِالتَّقْدِيم وَالتَّأخِيرِ . اهـ (١) . قَوْلُهُ : (مُقَدَّمٌ ، وَتَالِي يَعْنِي : أَنَّ الجُزْء الأَوَّلَ يُسَمَّى : مُقَدَّماً»، وَالجُزْةَ الثَّانِي يُسَمَّى : «تَالِياً». الأنبابي : قوله : (لِأَنَّ المَعْنَى لَا يَخْتَلِفُ ... إلخ) أي: فلا ترتيب بين جزأيها في المعنى، بل في الذكر فقط وقد يُقالُ : قد يكون بينهما ترتيب معنوي، كما إذا كان الحكم في أحدهما إثباتاً لشيء، وفي الآخر نفياً له، فإنَّ رتبة إثباته مقدَّمة على رتبة نفيه ؛ إذ لا يعقل سلب شيء إلا بعد تعقُله كما تقدَّم مراراً ؛ نحو: «هَذَا الشَّبَحُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ إِنْسَاناً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ غَيْرَ إِنْسَانِ». ويمكن أن يجاب بأنَّ الحصر إضافي؛ أي: بالنسبة للعِناد؛ أي: أنَّ التَّرتيب ليس إلا في الذكر، لا في العناد أو المنفي الترتيب المعنوي اللازم في كلِّ منفصلة، فافهم، أفاده الصَّبَّان . وناقشه العطّار : بأن قوله : «قد يكون بينهما ترتيب معنوي ... إلخ» لا يصح، بل لا يعقل؛ إذ مِنَ المعلوم أنَّ أداة الانفصال ربطت القضيَّتين وصيَّرتهما واحدة، كما اعترف به هو في غير هذا المحل، وحينئذ فكيف يعقل أنَّ في كلِّ مِنَ القضيَّتين حكماً بإثبات أو نفي؟ وإِنَّما الحكم بالتّنافي بين الطرفين، ولأنَّا لو نظرنا لِمَا اشتمل عليه الطَّرفان قبل الرَّبط ، ولاحظناه ،بعد ، لزم أنَّ كلَّ قضيَّةٍ شرطيَّةٍ مشتملةٌ على أحكام ثلاثة، ولم يقل بذلك أحد. قال السيد في حواشي القطب : إنَّ أطراف الشَّرطيَّة ليست قضايا ؛ لأنَّ القضيَّة لا تتم إلَّا إذا اعتبر فيها الحكم إيقاعاً أو انتزاعاً، وما اعتبر فيه ذلك لا يرتبط بغيره ضرورةً، فإنَّك إذا قلت : «الشَّمس طالعة» وأوقعت النِّسْبَة بين طرفيه، لم يتصور ربطه بشيءٍ آخر ؛ بأن يصيّر محكوماً عليه أو به، فما لم تتجرَّد القضيَّة عن الحكم، لم يمكن جعلها جزء قضيَّةٍ أخرى [اهـ]، وإذا تأمَّلت هذا حقَّ التَّأمُّل ظهر لك مصداق ما قلنا، وفي المحشي من أمثال هذا أشياء كثيرة، ولولا خوف ملل النَّاظر وتشويش الخاطر، لاستقصيناه. [اهـ كلام العطار ، ولا يخفى على النّبيه رده .
The two parts of it: antecedent and consequent. As for the explanation of the nature of the conditional: Al-Bajuri said: 'And Ibn Ya'qub also stated it explicitly when he said: The well-known in technical terminology is that the antecedent is the part entered by the conditional particle in the conjunctive conditional, and the consequent is what is attached to the part entered by it. As for the disjunctive conditional, it has no antecedent and no consequent; because the meaning does not differ with regard to it by advance or delay. End. His statement: (antecedent and consequent) means: the first part is called 'antecedent' and the second part is called 'consequent'.' Al-Anbabi said: 'His statement: (because the meaning does not differ ... etc.) means: there is no order between its two parts in meaning, but only in mention. And it might be said: sometimes there is a meaningful order between them, as when the judgment in one of them is an affirmation of something and in the other a negation of it; for the status of affirming it is prior to the status of negating it, since the negation of a thing is not conceivable except after its conceptualization, as has been stated repeatedly; e.g., "This figure: either it is a human or it is not a human." And it can be answered that the restriction is relative; i.e., with respect to opposition; i.e., that order is only in mention, not in opposition, or that the necessary meaningful order is denied in every disjunctive conditional. Understand, as al-Sabban conveyed it. And al-Attar criticized him: saying, 'His statement: "sometimes there is a meaningful order between them ... etc." is not valid, rather it is inconceivable; because it is known that the particle of disjunction linked the two propositions and made them one, as he himself admitted elsewhere. In that case, how can it be conceived that in each of the two propositions there is a judgment of affirmation or negation? Rather the judgment is about the mutual exclusivity between the two sides. And because if we look at what the two sides contain before the linking, and then consider it afterwards, it would entail that every conditional proposition contains three judgments, and no one has said that.' Al-Sayyid said in the footnotes of al-Qutb: 'The parts of a conditional proposition are not propositions; because a proposition is not complete unless judgment is considered in it as occurrence or extraction, and what is considered as such cannot be linked to another necessarily. For if you say, "The sun is rising" and you place the relation between its two ends, it is not conceivable to link it to something else by making it a subject or predicate. So as long as the proposition is not stripped of judgment, it cannot be made part of another proposition.' [End]. And if you reflect on this properly, the truth of what we said will appear to you. In the Hashiyah there are many things like this, and were it not for fear of boring the reader and confusing the mind, we would have exhausted it. [End of al-Attar's speech, and its refutation is not hidden from the intelligent.]
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage defines the technical terms 'muqaddam' (antecedent) and 'tali' (consequent) for conditional propositions, and clarifies their application to the two types of conditionals: muttasila (conjunctive) and munfasila (disjunctive). The author begins by citing Ibn Ya'qub, who states the well-known technical definitions: in a muttasila, the muqaddam is the part that follows the conditional particle (e.g., 'if'), and the tali is that which is suspended on it. In a munfasila, however, there is no muqaddam or tali because reversing the order does not change the meaning; e.g., 'Either it is a human or it is not a human' has the same meaning if reversed. Al-Bajuri then clarifies that the matn's phrase 'juzāhumā: muqaddamun wa tālin' means that the first part is called muqaddam and the second tali. Al-Anbabi then addresses a potential objection: it might be thought that in some munfasila there is a meaningful order, such as when one part affirms something and the other negates it, because affirmation is conceptually prior to negation. This objection is answered by noting that the restriction is relative to opposition (al-'inād): the order is only in verbal mention, not in the opposition itself. Alternatively, the necessary meaningful order is denied in every munfasila. Al-Attar criticizes the objection itself, arguing that the disjunction particle makes the two parts a single proposition with a single judgment of mutual exclusivity, so it is inconceivable to speak of separate judgments in each part. The passage then quotes al-Sayyid, explaining that the parts of a conditional are not independent propositions because linking a complete proposition to another is impossible. This technical discussion guards against misunderstanding the structure of disjunctive conditionals, which are symmetrical and do not have a fixed antecedent-consequent relationship.

Key Terms
مُقَدَّم
تَالِي
مُتَّصِلَة
مُنْفَصِلَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the technical terminology of logicians, which of the following is true about the disjunctive conditional (munfasila)?
A) It has a clear antecedent and consequent based on order.
B) It has no muqaddam or tali because meaning does not change with order.
C) It only has a muqaddam but no tali.
D) It is a type of conjunctive conditional.

Key Takeaway
In muttasila, the order of parts determines muqaddam and tali; in munfasila, order is irrelevant, so those terms do not apply.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following conditional from usul al-fiqh: 'If the evidence is definitive, then the ruling is certain.' Identify the muqaddam and tali, and explain why it is a muttasila.
?
Write a disjunctive conditional (munfasila) from a fiqh context, e.g., about the permissibility of an action, and explain why it has no muqaddam or tali.

The Six Types of Certain Premises
Bajuri defines the six types of certain premises with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the six types of certain premises (al-yaqiniyyat) and provide its definition and an example from the Islamic tradition as given by Bajuri.

Core Idea
Certain premises are six: first principles, observations, empirical, recurrent reports, intuitive, and sensed.

In this lesson, we will explore the six types of certain premises (al-yaqiniyyat) that form the foundation of demonstrative knowledge in Islamic logic. Bajuri defines each type with clear examples, allowing us to recognize them in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines
Start with the matn: 'Min awwaliyat mushahadat mujarrabat mutawatirat wa haddasiyyat wa mahsusat.' This lists the six types. Note the Arabic terms.
Awwaliyat = self-evident; Mushahadat = internal observation; etc.
2
Match each type to its definition
Bajuri defines mujarrabat as known by repetition yielding certainty, mutawatirat as known from non-colluding reporters, haddasiyyat as known by intuition, and mahsusat as known by external senses. For awwaliyat and mushahadat, recall definitions from earlier texts: immediate without medium; internal sense.
Definitions are provided for only four types in this passage.
3
Associate each type with its example
Examples: Scammony purges yellow bile (mujarrabat), Prophet's miracles (mutawatirat), moonlight from sun (haddasiyyat), sun rising (mahsusat). Internalize these to remember the types.
Examples are from medicine, theology, astronomy, and daily observation.
4
Note the debate on certainty
Bajuri reports that some scholars question whether mujarrabat, mutawatirat, and haddasiyyat are truly certain or merely probable. This is a key nuance.
The debate is expanded in the next lesson.
